hand","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-03-13T09:29:01Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"9492afdc-9e5c-4845-a43a-6863f94d74d9","Id":"wwfuture/story/20150312-how-to-talk-online-with-only-touch","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-03-13T09:29:01Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20150312-how-to-talk-online-with-only-touch"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20150312-how-to-talk-online-with-only-touch","_id":"559a5e45157e584f77e20cd2"}],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-09-11T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Israel: Boot camp for start-up success","HeadlineShort":"Boot camp for start-up success","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Tel Aviv stands apart as a hub for tech innovation thanks to one single overriding influence: the Israeli armed forces.\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[{"Image":[],"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"gallery","Guid":"","Id":"","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:gallery:p01g3tq1","_id":"559a5ab6157e584f77e17fcc"}],"AssetImage":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":175786,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1g/3v/p01g3vf4.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Rethink Robotics' Baxter","SynopsisMedium":"Rethink Robotics' Baxter","SynopsisShort":"Rethink Robotics' Baxter","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1g/3v/p01g3vf4.jpg","Title":"baxter_976.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01g3vf4","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01g3vf4","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01g3vf4","_id":"559a5c46157e584f77e1b92b"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":null,"Description":"","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Walter Frick","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"ebadf99a-9f33-4f1c-b3a2-d871deccf1b4","Id":"wwfuture/author/walter-frick","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:15Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"walter-frick"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/walter-frick","_id":"559a5a55157e584f77e1718e"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Five years ago, Rodney Brooks was looking to found a new robotics company, one that he believed could revolutionise the role of robots in our lives. And he knew there was only one place in the world to do it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A former professor of robotics at MIT and co-founder of the highly successful Massachusetts-based company iRobot, Brooks had spent enough time in Boston to recognise its dominance in his field. &ldquo;There is nowhere else in the world with such a concentration of academic robotics programs,&rdquo; says Brooks, whose latest company is &lt;a href=\"http://www.rethinkrobotics.com/\"&gt;Rethink Robotics&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;There also is nowhere else in the world with such a concentration of existing entrepreneurial robotics companies. Silicon Valley does not come close. New York does not come close. Pittsburgh does not come close. Nowhere in Europe comes close. Nowhere in Japan comes close.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Boston has always made the shortlist of US cities that excel in technology and entrepreneurship. But in recent years, the common perception is that the city has fallen behind. Silicon Valley has long since surpassed it in size, and New York City is becoming a hotbed of start-up activity. Even newer tech hubs such as Los Angeles and Boulder, Colorado, are seen by some as having more momentum.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Boston, meanwhile, has become more famous for being the place where young entrepreneurs like Mark Zuckerberg (Facebook) and Drew Houston (Dropbox) started their companies, before deciding to move to California to make them bigger. Even the well-known start-up accelerator &lt;a href=\"http://ycombinator.com/\"&gt;YCombinator&lt;/a&gt; started in Boston, before picking up and moving west to become a pillar of Silicon Valley culture.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But that view of Boston, though common in the tech press, misses both the lasting strength of the city&rsquo;s innovation scene, and its recent evolution toward urbanisation, collaboration, and risk-taking. Boston is a place with a history of expertise in science, healthcare, robotics, big data, and software. And for entrepreneurs like Brooks, it offers a tech scene unlike any other.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Deep and mature&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the 1970s and 80s, Boston became a computing industry hub, home to leading companies like Digital Equipment, Wang, and Data General. But the tech cluster that grew outside the city around the &nbsp;Route 128 highway famously missed the start of the PC revolution. And so by the 1990s, Silicon Valley had left Boston firmly in its dust.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet, the ingredients that had propelled Boston&rsquo;s strength in technology remained: world-class research universities, a highly educated population, and a high concentration of venture capital. (Boston can also claim the creation of the first formal venture capital firm, &lt;a href=\"http://www.boston.com/business/articles/2008/04/06/venture_capitals_grandfather/?page=full\"&gt;American Research and Development&lt;/a&gt;, which was founded in 1946.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fred Destin of Atlas Venture describes the city&rsquo;s innovation ecosystem as being deep and mature. &ldquo;It's probably the only true &lsquo;generalist&rsquo; ecosystem outside of the Bay Area,&rdquo; he says. A &lt;a href=\"http://blog.startupcompass.co/pages/entrepreneurship-ecosystem-report\"&gt;report&lt;/a&gt; last year by the Startup Genome, an initiative to catalogue start-up statistics globally, seems to agree. Though the report notes that Boston now trails New York in the size of its ecosystem, it highlights the former&rsquo;s diversity, concluding that &ldquo;the Boston start-up ecosystem has the same healthy mix of start-ups&hellip;as [Silicon Valley].&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The breadth of the city&rsquo;s innovation can be attributed in large part to Greater Boston&rsquo;s eight research universities, including Harvard and MIT. Together, they bring in &lt;a href=\"http://web.mit.edu/newsoffice/2003/econimpact.html\"&gt;$1.5bn (&pound;1bn) annually&lt;/a&gt; in research grants and contracts, a significant amount of which translates into innovative technologies and ideas that eventually fuel the creation of start-ups. &ldquo;Concentration of universities is a big contributor to that entrepreneurial environment,&rdquo; says Gordon Jones, managing director of &lt;a href=\"http://ilab.harvard.edu/\"&gt;Harvard&rsquo;s Innovation Lab&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;The size of the city relative to the concentration of universities makes it very palpable.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While the universities have always been seen as a key source of new technologies, today their role in producing student entrepreneurs is equally important. Spurred in part by a fear of missing out on the next Zuckerberg, venture capitalists are actively courting students and their dorm-room start-ups. General Catalyst, one of the younger of Boston&rsquo;s premier VC firms, has gone so far as to formalise this effort by creating &lt;a href=\"http://bostinno.streetwise.co/2012/12/12/rough-draft-ventures-general-catalyst-funds-student-startups/\"&gt;Rough Draft Ventures&lt;/a&gt;, a mini-VC fund backed by the firm but run by students. With Rough Draft, General Catalyst brought together the city&rsquo;s most talented student entrepreneurs, and tasked them with making small investments in their peers. The fund has made several such investments so far, and in July, General Catalyst &lt;a href=\"http://bostinno.streetwise.co/all-series/harvard-alum-peter-boyce-joins-general-catalyst-partners/\"&gt;hired Peter Boyce&lt;/a&gt;, a graduating member of the initial Rough Draft team, as an associate.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Universities are also expanding their support for entrepreneurship, with ideas like Harvard&rsquo;s i-Lab, which functions as both an incubator for student start-ups and as a single resource centre to connect entrepreneurial activities across campus. This echoes MIT&rsquo;s pioneering &lt;a href=\"http://media.mit.edu/\"&gt;Media Lab&lt;/a&gt;, which was set up in 1985 and has long been encouraging entrepreneurship in areas ranging from wearable computing to stackable electric cars for cities.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Academic strengths&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The other big difference between today and the Route 128 days is the &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticcities.com/jobs-and-economy/2013/08/how-east-coast-tech-startups-became-downtown-phenomenon/6301/\"&gt;shifting centre of gravity&lt;/a&gt; from the suburbs to the city. Kendall Square, the home of MIT in neighbouring Cambridge and a long-time start-up hub, has exploded with activity in the past several years. &ldquo;You can't walk down the street in Kendall Square, Cambridge, without running into venture capitalists, Nobel laureates, start-up CEOs,&rdquo; says Tim Rowe, founder of the Kendall-based &lt;a href=\"http://cic.us/\"&gt;Cambridge Innovation Center&lt;/a&gt;. Rowe&rsquo;s CIC, founded in 1999, is a big part of that. It has served as an early incubator for many of Boston&rsquo;s current crop of growing tech start-ups, including HubSpot, which makes marketing software. HubSpot spent four years in the CIC from 2004, growing to nearly &lt;a href=\"http://cic.us/2010/08/after-four-great-years-cic-says-thank-you-and-goodbye-to-hubspot/\"&gt;200 employees&lt;/a&gt; by the time it moved to its own office space. As of the end of 2012, the company claimed &lt;a href=\"http://www.hubspot.com/blog/bid/34205/HubSpot-Continues-Rapid-Trajectory-with-82-Revenue-Growth\"&gt;$52.5m (&pound;33.9m) in annual revenue and 450 employees&lt;/a&gt;; it is expected to go public in the next year.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Kendall is not the only beneficiary of Boston&rsquo;s increasingly urbanised tech cluster. An &ldquo;Innovation District&rdquo; championed by Mayor Thomas Menino has attracted young tech companies - including Rethink Robotics- &nbsp;and more established innovators like Zipcar and EnerNOC. Many VC firms have also relocated from the suburbs, opening offices in Boston or Cambridge. And most of these mini-clusters are connected by the &lt;a href=\"http://bostinno.streetwise.co/2013/07/01/branding-boston-tech-how-about-just-the-red-line/\"&gt;red line&lt;/a&gt; of the city&rsquo;s subway system, making them easy to travel to without a car.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite these shifts, some things about Boston&rsquo;s tech scene are unchanged. It remains comparatively strong in areas where it can flex its academic muscles, like &lt;a href=\"http://www.bostonglobe.com/business/2013/08/24/why-magnet-for-life-sciences/aGZ9zxinynXfEdDy0OfRYM/story.html\"&gt;healthcare and biotech&lt;/a&gt;, robotics, and big data. Its successes in software have been concentrated more in business solutions - Endeca, ITA Software, Demandware, now HubSpot - than the consumer technologies that tend to garner more attention. That mix is in stark contrast to New York, which is more narrowly &lt;a href=\"http://bostinno.streetwise.co/2012/10/22/the-real-difference-between-boston-and-new-yorks-tech-sectors/\"&gt;concentrated in web and mobile software&lt;/a&gt; startups. But while the two cities are intense rivals when it comes to sports, their geographical closeness is creating a &lt;a href=\"http://bostinno.streetwise.co/2013/04/29/the-bolt-bus-economy-a-boston-reporters-trip-to-silicon-alley\"&gt;significant degree of collaboration&lt;/a&gt;, with investors splitting time between the two cities or opening satellite offices.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;New York&rsquo;s advantage is its size, as well as its status as a global leader in both finance and culture. As Destin put it: &ldquo;[Boston&rsquo;s] main weakness lies in the fact that it is not a great aspirational city or region, in the way that New York, London or the Bay Area can be. Young people come to study at its great institutions then leave. If Boston became aspirational in terms of culture and lifestyle it could take the next step up,&rdquo; he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One related challenge the Boston tech community wrestles with is how to tell its own story. &ldquo;Boston doesn't hype its success,&rdquo; says Eric Paley, managing partner at the VC firm Founder Collective. &ldquo;But it is a community that has consistently built big technology companies.&rdquo; In a city with less well-known media than New York, and fewer tech-specific publications than Silicon Valley, getting the word out can be difficult&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sometimes, at least, the sheer novelty of the innovation is enough to overcome that obstacle. In September, 2012, Brooks and the Rethink Robotics team unveiled its flagship product: &lt;a href=\"http://www.rethinkrobotics.com/products/overview/\"&gt;Baxter&lt;/a&gt;, a manufacturing robot designed to work side-by-side with humans, able to be taught new tasks without any programming. By that time, Brooks had &lt;a href=\"http://www.xconomy.com/boston/2012/09/18/rod-brooks-and-rethink-reveal-an-industrial-robot-for-the-masses/\"&gt;raised $62m&lt;/a&gt; (&pound;40m) in venture capital, from both Boston and West Coast firms. The launch caught the attention of media across the country, garnering &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2012/08/26/opinion/sunday/i-made-the-robot-do-it.html\"&gt;multiple&lt;/a&gt; &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2012/09/18/science/a-robot-with-a-delicate-touch.html?pagewanted=all\"&gt;pieces&lt;/a&gt; in The New York Times. For a moment, at least, the world remembered what Boston&rsquo;s tech ecosystem does.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-09-04T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Boston: The start-up revolution reviving its tech cred","HeadlineShort":"Boston: Restoring its tech cred?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Boston was once Americas most thriving tech hub. But Silicon Valleys ascendancy tarnished its reputation. Could a rash of new start-ups signal a resurgence?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Sitting on 400 hectares of the most expensive Russian real estate outside central Moscow, Skolkovo is intended to be one of the biggest tech innovation centres in the world. In 2010, then-president Dmitry Medvedev gave orders to create an innovative centre from scratch &ndash; this in a country where all tech parks and scientific centres have been inherited from the Soviet past. &ldquo;We have money but don&rsquo;t have our Silicon Valley,&rdquo; he said on his visit to Silicon Valley, after earlier stressing it was a project &ldquo;that should become the largest test ground for Russian new economic policy.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But at the moment plans for an internationally renowned tech hub a half-hour&rsquo;s drive from Red Square are still a dream. The fantasy starts with the idea that it takes half an hour to drive there through Moscow&rsquo;s notoriously heavy traffic; on a weekday it takes a few hours. When you reach there you are greeted with signs saying &ldquo;Skolkovo - we are making the future today&rdquo;, but beyond the perimeter fence lies a couple of cranes and an excavator digging the foundations of this future fantasy. Several hundred metres away stands the only finished building, called Hypercube; it is supposed to be an example of the eco-friendly principles intended for Skolkovo. Looking at the building from the dusty and polluted highway, however, makes you wonder just how green the enterprise will truly be.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Three years after its announcement by President Medvedev, Skolkovo still raises more questions than answers. Getting Skolkovo off the ground has not been cheap, with billions of oil dollars spent, and yet more to be allocated. There are doubts that the Russian government &ndash; now headed by Vladimir Putin &ndash; has the conviction to keep going. The investments are impressive though. The road to be built to Skolkovo and around the area is said to cost $2 billion. However, the biggest questions facing start-ups that have, or want to, set up shop there is where they are going to work and live. The whole site is supposed to be finished by 2020.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One source close to Skolkovo said that the project is modeled on tech centres such as &lt;a href=\"http://www.sophia-antipolis.org/\"&gt;Sophia Antipolis&lt;/a&gt; in France, &lt;a href=\"http://www.cyberjaya-msc.com/\"&gt;Cyberjaya&lt;/a&gt; in Malaysia or &lt;a href=\"http://www.masdar.ae/en/\"&gt;Masdar&lt;/a&gt; in UAE. While the start-ups wait for the so needed infrastructure to be finished in 2015-16, they are being sent to other scientific centres to carry on their projects. Skolkovo&rsquo;s vice-president for external communications and advertising, Alexander Chernov, has tried to assure the press everything is fine and will be finished soon. &ldquo;It is a usual trend with construction business in Russia. Two years shift is not that bad but we will welcome our residents in 2015. If not, we will look like losers.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Russia has a long history of scientific and technological breakthroughs, but it is suffering from a hangover from recent decades. The Cold War and the Iron Curtain played a key role in maintaining a competitive and innovative spirit in closed science cities of the Soviet Union, such as &lt;a href=\"http://novosibirskguide.com/Akademgorodok/\"&gt;Akademgorodok&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Core disciplines&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But after the Cold War, everything changed. The new Russia found itself with the remnants of the once powerful Soviet scientific machine and a huge brain drain in the direction of the US. Innovative ideas started to matter very little in a new capitalist society focused on selling Russia&rsquo;s vast natural resources and cashing in US dollars. It took two decades for a project like Skolkovo to emerge from the Kremlin&rsquo;s corridors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what will Skolkovo concentrate on? There are five core areas: information technology, nuclear technology, energy efficiency, biomedical innovation, and space and telecommunications. Companies receiving resident status can work on their projects and attempt to bring them to market. Skolkovo&rsquo;s grants and construction are mostly backed up by the money from the Russian government. The investment is supposed to reach over $15bn (&pound;10bn) by 2020. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the past couple of years, Skolkovo has received over 10,000 online applications. Applicants range from companies and startup groups to scientific centres, universities, student groups and distinguished scientists. Most applicants fail to become residents. &ldquo;The main challenge here is either a project doesn&rsquo;t fit into the five clusters or it hasn&rsquo;t been worked through. Sometimes companies overestimate their abilities or their idea has already been developed in the market,&rdquo; says Chernov.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Currently, more than 900 Skolkovo residents or startup companies are working on projects across the five clusters. Many of them have already received grants from the Skolkovo Fund said to expect to be worth $2bn (&pound;1.28bn) by 2019.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the grant recipients is &lt;a href=\"http://www.fruct.org/\"&gt;Fruct&lt;/a&gt; founded by Russian and Finnish universities together with Nokia. Although they won the money last year, they should receive the $300,000 (&pound;191,000) grant this autumn to develop a mobile device which monitors eight key diagnostic markers such as cardiovascular, pulmonology and endocrinologic readings, to help monitor potential health problems such as arterial hypertension arrhythmia, asthma, and &nbsp;diabetes.. Asked about how they will spend the money, the head of Fruct, Sergey Balandin, says: &ldquo;The challenge was to make investors believe in our project. We will put more emphasis on the research now. We will try to improve the weak sides of our project with the help of Skolkovo Fund&rsquo;s specialists and prepare the product to enter the market.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other hopefuls have not been so lucky. &lt;a href=\"http://www.bravomotors.ru/\"&gt;Bravo Motors&lt;/a&gt; applied for the grant to finance its project for an ultra-light electric vehicle called the e-Trike. The main challenge for Bravo now is to find $1.4 m (&pound;890,000) in investment to complete the development of the e-Trike, carry out bench and field testing, and start production. But to get tax incentives, the company had to change the e-Trike&rsquo;s colour from red to green &ndash; the same colour used on Skolkovo&rsquo;s logo. Being part of Skolkovo may mean compromises.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Distrustful politicians &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Skolkovo has not been without rumours of corruption, either. Medvedev tried to avoid corruption by appointing Russian oligarch Victor Vekselberg as the President of Skolkovo. But 2013 started with several cases. &lt;a href=\"http://www.skoltech.ru/\"&gt;Skoltech University&lt;/a&gt; is being created to educate in-house innovators of tomorrow. MIT is heavily involved with the project, helping Skoltech with the structure, educational and research programs. The university hasn&rsquo;t been built yet &ndash; but already it has created a corruption scandal. The state audit office found out recently that millions of dollars allocated to Skolkovo&rsquo;s Skoltech, went, instead, to finance programs and grants at MIT. The government tried to bury the story, but &lt;a href=\"http://izvestia.ru/news/548178\"&gt;Russian newspaper Izvestia&lt;/a&gt; got hold of the case documents&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then there is the political situation to contend with. Even Skolkovo&rsquo;s biggest cheerleaders understand start-ups like the e-Trike won&rsquo;t be able to make a return on the multi-billion-dollar investments poured into the project. Russian politicians, in general, are distrustful of innovations they don&rsquo;t understand such as a recent discussion at Skolkovo on the creation perpetuum mobile.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A source close to Skolkovo who wished to remain anonymous, says: &nbsp;&ldquo;The success of Skolkovo should be assessed on the basis of whether it manages to create a huge and successful business like Yandex [Russia&rsquo;s biggest search engine] and Mail.ru [Russian email].&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The challenge for Skolkovo is to prove it can make money, rather than just spend it. It has been created from the very top of the political spectrum - with huge investments &ndash; but the buildings remain unconstructed, and the ideas that could make it profitable remain untapped.&nbsp; The question is, will this always be the case?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-28T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Skolkovo: Tech city that aims to restore nation's pride","HeadlineShort":"Russia: Restoring its tech pride?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In the Soviet era, Russian science was lavished with money and resources. But post-communism, innovation has slumped. Can a new tech city reverse its fortunes?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"In the Soviet era, Russian science was lavished with money and resources. But post-communism, innovation has slumped. Can a new tech city reverse its fortunes?","SummaryShort":"Is Russias tech hub doomed to fail?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:50Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"af10d13d-4217-4fcf-a3c5-a605d9f8a7a9","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130827-skolkovo-too-little-too-late","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:50Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130827-skolkovo-too-little-too-late"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130827-skolkovo-too-little-too-late","_id":"559a5e0e157e584f77e20651"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Japanese start-up entrepreneur Masayoshi Hashimoto believes he knows where the country&rsquo;s IT industry is getting its guidance from; the Czech surrealist author and satirist Franz Kafka.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Working for a Japanese web services firm, I couldn&rsquo;t believe how badly things were done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;We had everyone managing projects using Excel. Excel, I ask you! It beggars belief. But managers here like the old ways.&rdquo; When changes were suggested he was stonewalled.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Venting his irritation on Twitter led him to a conversation about the business practices afflicting Japan&rsquo;s tech sector with a fellow malcontent. One face-to-face meeting later, he quit his job to form a company with his new comrade rebel with and created the website &lt;a href=\"http://www.paradiseware.net\"&gt;Paradiseware&lt;/a&gt;, which &lt;a href=\"https://app.mmth.tw/?lang=en\"&gt;helps businesses manage projects more efficiently&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The result is typically Japanese; run on a shoestring without any of the benefits Silicon Valley start-ups might attract.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan has a real problem with innovation. It&rsquo;s just not very Japanese. Nor, you might be surprised to read, is originality. It&rsquo;s the kind of thing swaggering foreigners or suspect loners are thought to meddle in. So if you visit Japan and find ersatz statues of Liberty in tourist spots, or a very &lt;a href=\"http://www.cooljapan.org/\"&gt;uncool bureaucratic drive&lt;/a&gt; to promote Japanese creativity and culture ripped off from Cool Britannia - you&rsquo;ll begin to understand why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan is \"good at technology but poor in business\" as one official puts it. &ldquo;Its high levels of basic research and superior technology are hampered by too-strict regulations, and vertically integrated administration. The gap between researchers and manufacturers, in many cases, prevents brilliant R&amp;D results from being put into practical use - the &lsquo;Death Valley&rsquo; problem.&rdquo; Japan leads in its high number of early adopters. Thanks to them, inventors can gain confidence and the initial momentum needed to push off a new idea. But it often doesn&rsquo;t last long.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Unfortunately this is accompanied by a very envious, critical and vocal audience. They underline every possible reason that the product will fail and keep doing so indefatigably. They attack the innovator on Twitter, Facebook, blogs, etc; and they keep doing so until the innovator raises a white flag.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Add to this 20 year&rsquo;s economic stagnation, zero appetite for risk and eroded confidence and Japan emerges as one the toughest environments on Earth to root new ideas.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Struggling start-ups&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are backers in Japan, his partner Shuku Tanizaki explains, but most are interested only in no-risk ventures. Start-ups have to attract crowd-funding or, as is more usual, the personal savings of their creators. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s lots of venture capitalists here but they are only interested in games and new media,&rdquo; says Hashimoto. Even renting conventional office space can be troublesome in Japan - landlords like to see two years&rsquo; profits before they open the door. Paradiseware operates out of cheap, shared office space in central Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think starting a new business means also confronting the problems afflicting your society,&rdquo; says Mr Hashimoto.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite being perceived as a big-hitting innovator, Japan has a tiny, struggling start-up landscape that has yet to make an impact abroad. &ldquo;This is because this sector is the most under-supported in Japan,&rdquo; says Terrie Lloyd, an Australian entrepreneur and commentator based in Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As the global digital economy grows in importance, this puts Japan, the world&rsquo;s third-biggest economy and one-time electronics giant, at a massive disadvantage. America and even tiny South Korea continue to come up with the gadgets and smartphone-based services that consumers want. Japan&rsquo;s sluggish megacorporations such as Sony are unable to match the swift-footed foreign competition and fall further and further behind.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What strangles originality here, explains Tokyo-based technology consultant Nobuyuki Hayashi, is that Japanese innovation keeps to very rigid structures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Most of them went to the same university, studied the same subjects and never met people from outside the group. This is because of the Japanese educational system and how Japanese recruiting is done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Japanese companies tend to hire graduates from the same school.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This spectacular failure to respond to the internet age is slowly being challenged, however, both by the country&rsquo;s new government and a few entrepreneurs. Copying ideas from Silicon Valley, top entrepreneurs and officials have tried for over a decade now to spark some life into Japan&rsquo;s hi-tech sector by increasing incubation projects and the like. Japan now has over 400 business incubators up from 30 in 1999 some of which are focused on the hi-tech sector. Most offer funding, work space and mentoring.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And after being held back by the entropy of their elders for so long, Japan&rsquo;s enterprising young are going it alone. An experienced user interface designer in Japan and abroad, 26-year-old Alexander Takahashi simply rents shared office space, and networks for Angel investors when he can, to develop a location-based service named &lt;a href=\"https://itunes.apple.com/us/app/rooftops/id552140477?mt=8\"&gt;Rooftops&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He understands the reservations. &ldquo;Japanese businessmen tend not to trust the younger generation. No matter how well you present yourself - young equals bad,&rdquo; says Takahashi.&nbsp; &ldquo;And honestly I can understand them. There are no really new start-ups with new ideas nowadays.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japanese young entrepreneurs are enthusiastic but lack crucial business know-how, says Lloyd. &ldquo;They are totally unprepared for the business world, and this is why cloistered accelerators and incubators are so popular here,&rdquo; he adds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hitoshi Masuda, who until recently headed up the government&rsquo;s small-business think tank, disagrees. He says: &ldquo;Japan is changing profoundly but dynamically in terms of who drives innovation and how innovation is being driven.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Economic reforms under a new government - Abenomics, named for the PM Shinzo Abe - are having some positive effects. His party has also promised to fight the corner of all innovators, risk takers and creative types.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their model is a bunch of maverick, by Japanese standards, entrepreneurs which include Japan&rsquo;s third-richest man, Masayoshi Son, born to a Korean immigrant family, whose mobile phone empire Softbank is funding a brave attempt at commercialising home-grown green tech innovations. His outsider outlook was a huge benefit, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;If you look at successful Japanese start-ups, they are mostly run by CEOs who have applied the &lsquo;foreign connection&rsquo; in some way - be it education, money, technology, business deals, or talent,&rdquo; says Lloyd. &ldquo;In some cases, like Mr Son, he has managed to combine all five factors.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those factors seem to have inoculated such champions against fear of failure making them bold at the same time. They represent a new way of being Japanese &ndash; one the nation urgently needs to get to grips with.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-21T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Can Japan reboot its anti-innovation start-up culture?","HeadlineShort":"Can Japan plug its start-up gap?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Japan has long been hailed as one of the worlds most developed nations. But when it comes to innovation, things are less rosy. Why is it stifling its start-ups?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Japan has long been hailed as one of the worlds most developed nations. But its start-up culture is falling behind. How can it bounce back?","SummaryShort":"Tackling the countrys lack of innovation","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"c9500398-ed51-4dc1-8694-e64d1f783cc6","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap","_id":"559a5e0d157e584f77e2063b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When new businesses based on the computer chip began to cluster in Santa Clara Valley, it did not take long for someone to come up with the name Silicon Valley. That set a pattern, and soon there was &lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/business/3249891.stm\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Glen&lt;/a&gt; in Scotland, Kenya&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-21173158\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Savannah&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://www.silicongulf.net/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Gulf&lt;/a&gt; in the Philippines, &lt;a href=\"http://www.academpark.com/en/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Taiga&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia &ndash; even the somewhat less romantic-sounding &lt;a href=\"http://www.siliconroundabout.org.uk/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Roundabout&lt;/a&gt; in London.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now there is Silicon Allee in Berlin (Allee, meaning alley or avenue in German, is part of many street names in the city), which to some observers evokes the atmosphere of the early days in Northern California. That was certainly the vibe at &lt;a href=\"http://toaberlin.com/\"&gt;Tech Open Air Berlin&lt;/a&gt;, a gathering held earlier this month in a ramshackle complex of wooden and stone buildings in Kreuzberg, a trendy part of the city. There was soft acoustic music and falafel, and a lot of beautiful people exuding a studied coolness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to see a lot of amazing stuff coming out of Berlin,&rdquo; says Tikhon Bernstam, who came over from Silicon Valley to attend the event. &ldquo;This sort of culture that you see here, the hippy music, we had exactly the same thing in San Francisco a few years ago. I&rsquo;ve been impressed by the quality of the companies I&rsquo;ve been seeing here.&rdquo; Bernstam&rsquo;s cloud data service he co-founded, Parse, was recently bought by Facebook for $85 million.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, Berlin is many leagues behind Silicon Valley. From Adobe through to Yahoo, via Apple, Intel, Google and Oracle, the Valley, as its occupants call it, has a combined wealth which most countries would envy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exact figures are hard to come by, but Berlin came ninth in &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticcities.com/jobs-and-economy/2013/06/new-global-start-cities/5144/\"&gt;a recent global ranking&lt;/a&gt; of cities with the most tech start-ups founded since 2002 that are global, or received angel funding or institutional venture capital. The top three spots were occupied by San Francisco-Silicon Valley with 201 start-ups, New York (144) and London (90). Berlin had 27, just ahead of Bangalore on 26 and Sao Paulo on 21.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But with its reputation for doing things differently, very cheap rents, and the ambition and optimism on show at events like Tech Open Air Berlin, the hope is that its scene will deliver on the buzz that has surrounded it for the past few years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The city itself is in many ways a start up. From 1945 it was shattered and split for almost half a century. The eastern, Soviet-controlled zone was hardly a seed-bed for innovation. Industry moved out from the West. Students were exempted from military service, attracting a young, artistic and politically radical alternative scene.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the fall of the Wall in 1989, the city has been re-inventing itself. The vibrant alternative culture remains in place, and some see it as providing fertile ground for the creation of disruptive technologies &ndash; though others argue that a counterculture-led antipathy to money-making may hamper innovation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One thing that is clear is that property is cheap &ndash; Berlin still has rents which are so low that they make visitors from London and New York gasp. The average annual cost of a workstation in an office in Berlin, including maintenance costs, taxes and rent, was $8,410 at the end of last year, compared with $14,050 for New York and $14,620 in the City of London, according to &lt;a href=\"http://dtz.b15media.com/downloads/DTZ_Occupier_Perspective_Global_Occupancy_Costs_Offices_2013.pdf\"&gt;a report&lt;/a&gt; published by the global property company DTZ. Renting a 120 square metre apartment in Berlin costs around a third what it does in London and New York.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This combination has attracted many who aspire to be the next big thing in tech. In a few cases they have already succeeded, such as the social game developer &lt;a href=\"http://www.wooga.com/\"&gt;Wooga&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/\"&gt;Research Gate&lt;/a&gt;, a networking website for scientists. The darling of the scene is &lt;a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/\"&gt;SoundCloud&lt;/a&gt;, an audio sharing platform that has 200 million users every month. Alex Ljung, its co-founder and chief executive, moved the company from Stockholm to Berlin because he felt it offered a richer mix of people involved in music, and because of the ample and cheap office space.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, Ljung admits his firm&rsquo;s new hometown doesn&rsquo;t cover all bases. &ldquo;With start-up life, there&rsquo;s never really much calmness. It&rsquo;s all jet and no lag,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;In an average month, I spend about a week-and-a-half in Berlin, about a week in San Francisco and the rest of the time split between Los Angeles, New York and London.&rdquo; What Berlin lacks that San Francisco has is the sheer volume of people who&rsquo;ve grown tech companies, where Ljung says he can bump into others who have trodden the same path, and who can advise him on short-cuts and pitfalls. In New York, London and Los Angeles, he can meet plenty of people with money to invest in good ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Bernstam, investors from the US are scared to invest in Germany because they don&rsquo;t understand the way companies are structured and corporate regulations. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t understand what could happen if future investors tried to dilute us,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;and investors from the States don&rsquo;t understand yet how that works abroad &ndash; and are frankly scared of investing abroad.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All of which created a chicken-and-egg problem in Berlin. Some entrepreneurs have stayed away because of the lack of investors, and those with the money have preferred to go elsewhere because of the greater number of companies to invest in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this resistance is beginning breaking down, on both sides, according to David Knight, editor-in-chief of Berlin tech scene blog &lt;a href=\"http://siliconallee.com/\"&gt;Silicon Allee&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s been this streak of creativity and once you got a core of people, which started round 2009, you started to get a lot of investors from the US and Europe looking to Berlin. It&rsquo;s still at a very early stage, but there&rsquo;s a lot more money now than there was two years ago.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Districts such as Kreuzberg, Neukoelln and Prenzlauer Berg, where would-be entrepreneurs congregate in the bars and cafes offering wi-fi, are now seeing the effect of this. In Prenzlauer Berg, &lt;a href=\"http://www.factoryberlin.com/\"&gt;Factory&lt;/a&gt;, which received &euro;1 million in funding from Google, has rented space to over 15 companies, including SoundCloud. The city also now has a lot more of what might be called serious business people. Florian Lanzer is trying to build a website selling green products to people who are not necessarily committed environmentalists. He said the project has attracted a lot of interest, including the involvement of a former CEO of Sony Germany.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;What Berlin gets right is that it attracts the right kind of people,&rdquo; says Lanzer. &ldquo;Young people with talent and people with long track records and massive experience are coming together.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around a decade ago, Berlin was famously described as being &ldquo;poor, but sexy&rdquo; by its mayor, Klaus Wowereit. The hope is that creativity and commerce is now beginning to combine in tech in such a way that it might finally shed its poor tag.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Berlin start-ups: Cool and commerce coming together?","HeadlineShort":"Berlin: Can cool and capital mix?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In a new series Innovation Cultures takes an on-the-ground look at tech scenes around the world that hope in some way to emulate the successes of Silicon Valley. First up is Berlin, where Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"In the first of a new series looking at global tech scenes, Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","SummaryShort":"Hip start-up city may be coming of age","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"4b0f1c8f-2f5c-472d-8d83-5265b87745f1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","_id":"559a5e0c157e584f77e2061d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Before last year, George Wainaina&rsquo;s tiny shop selling agricultural goods and veterinarian supplies in Karura, a lush farming area of central Kenya, wasn&rsquo;t doing so well. His customers found it difficult to get what they needed. Some would complain; others would simply leave.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t sell many products, I didn&rsquo;t have many suppliers, and I used to have problems with working capital,&rdquo; recalls Wainaina, the 27-year-old entrepreneur and professionally trained veterinarian. &ldquo;I would tell them to come back next time. Some used to come back, but most lost trust and disappeared.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;His problem, and that of the farmers he wanted to serve, cut straight to the heart of one of sub-Saharan Africa&rsquo;s deepest challenges when it comes to creating a profitable agricultural sector: access.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Africa is home to a quarter of the world&rsquo;s arable land &ndash; ripe and ready to become the world&rsquo;s breadbasket. Yet a stunning 80% of this is not being utilised. And of the 20% that is, the majority is owned by poor smallholder farmers with just one or two hectares of land, who face enormous challenges when it comes to accessing things to improve harvests and incomes that developed world farmers perhaps take for granted: credit; seeds; fertiliser; information on weather and market prices; and the markets to sell their products.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Radical overhaul&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But things are beginning to turn around. A wide range of innovations are being developed in Kenya to help increase farmers&rsquo; access to inputs, information and markets, and to boost their productivity.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since last July, Wainaina has been the proud owner of a &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://www.farmshop.co.ke/\"&gt;Farm Shop&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; store, joining a growing franchise network (locally owned and operated) of agro dealers located in rural and under-served areas of Kenya, which hopes to boost smallholder productivity by providing farmers with high-quality products, services, and information. Where once there was a haphazardly arranged set of goods hidden behind a metal cage, now his shop&rsquo;s aisles and shelves are neatly stocked with everything from fertilisers to hybrid seeds, insecticides, and micro-drip irrigation systems. The store holds demos, promotions, and free training sessions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the keys to Farm Shop&rsquo;s radical overhaul is that it has done away with the incredibly cumbersome, slow, and inefficient process of paper ledgers and stock keeping. In each store, owners are given an Android-powered tablet with software that allows them quickly and easily order inventory &ndash; all at the touch of a button. Point-of-sale transactions are now tracked in real-time, which gives the company a unique ability to track and respond to every single sale, as well as prices and inventory levels. &ldquo;Before, we used to use cash receipts and paper record books,&rdquo; says Wainaina. We can now trace all our sales, find totals easily, and order supplies much faster.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The effect has been almost immediate. Business, says Wainaina, has increased nearly five-fold since he converted the store. Last month, his Farm Shop branch saw an astounding 400,000 KES ($4,500) in sales, with over 1,350 transactions. Farm Shop currently has four shops across Kenya, offering 54 products and services like soil testing, and they are planning on opening eight new shops in eight weeks, starting this month.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The company is also offering to help farmers actually figure out what they need to improve their harvest. With their new soil-testing service, Farm Shop agents can collect soil samples from farmers, and within a few days send results directly to the farmers via SMS, informing them what will help improve yields. For example, if they detect a potassium deficiency, a farmer can then go to the shop and purchase more fertiliser, or get the right kind for what their soil needs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Farm Shop is not an answer to all farmers&rsquo; woes. As Julio De Souza, Farm Shop&rsquo;s COO admits, &ldquo;no individual solution can solve the problems for rural farmers in Africa. It&rsquo;s an ecosystem that needs to be built.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Hedging harvests&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That ecosystem, at least in Kenya, is slowly starting to develop. Several innovations like Farm Shop are improving the lives and income of Africa&rsquo;s struggling smallholder farmers, and across the entire value chain of rural agriculture.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For the past few years, social enterprise &lt;a href=\"http://kilimosalama.wordpress.com/\"&gt;Kilimo Salama&lt;/a&gt; has been piloting a crop insurance scheme for smallholder farmers, hoping to reduce risk and serve a critical and vulnerable market that traditional insurance schemes don&rsquo;t reach. &ldquo;Nobody ever really wakes up and says &lsquo;I want insurance&rsquo;,&rdquo; confesses Rose Goslinga, Technical Coordinator for Kilimo Salama. But, it&rsquo;s become a highly valuable tool for helping farmers secure much-needed credit from banks.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Traditionally, farmers try to reduce their exposure to risks like crop failure (from bad rains or crop parasites) by minimising their investment in farming inputs. As a consequence, farmers remain trapped in a cycle of low agricultural productivity and poverty. If the rains fail, they are left with nothing to invest in the next season.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Using weather stations across the country, Kilimo Salama has developed a unique &ldquo;weather-based index insurance,&rdquo; which farmers can buy into at the beginning of the season, for typically around 10-20% of the amount they invest in seeds and inputs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Weather stations are equipped with small sim-cards that wirelessly transmit data every five minutes to a cloud-based server. At the end of the season, this data is aggregated and coupled with satellite data, and used to map out rain patterns. Kilimo Salama then works with agronomists to calculate the index &ndash; and find where the rain was too much, too little, or at the wrong time. Farmer payouts are automatically calculated based on their crops, location, and number of seeds purchased.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;By coupling bank loans with Kilimo Salama&rsquo;s insurance scheme, the organisation has enabled banks and microfinance institutions to be more comfortable with giving loans, making access to essential credit easier for farmers. &ldquo;We not just selling insurance,&rdquo; says Goslinga, &ldquo;we&rsquo;re enabling farmers to get a harvest.&rdquo; As of this year, Kilimo Salama has insured over 100,000 smallholder farmers across Kenya and Rwanda.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;One SMS at a time&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet even if farmers have a great harvest, that doesn&rsquo;t mean it&rsquo;s easy for them to sell their produce. Calestous Juma, Harvard Professor and author of The New Harvest; Agricultural Innovation in Africa claims that the greatest failure of Africa&rsquo;s agricultural sector is the absence of investment in rural infrastructure. &ldquo;Markets cannot function if you cannot move goods, services, information and ideas,&rdquo; he says.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In a nutshell, the problem is many of Africa&rsquo;s rural farmers can&rsquo;t get their produce to the markets in time, because of bad roads, lack of communication, and the nature of a highly fractured middle-man-dominated agricultural market. Back in 2011, when nearly 3.7 million people in northern Kenya were facing near-famine conditions, farmers in other parts of the country were either driven by swindling middlemen to sell cabbage at nearly a tenth of the price, or waste it all together.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.mfarm.co.ke/\"&gt;MFarm&lt;/a&gt;, a Kenya-based tech startup, is trying to boost this sector, &ldquo;one SMS at a time.&rdquo; Sub-Saharan Africa has the fastest-growing mobile market in the world, increasing at an average of 44% annually since 2000, according to the worldwide mobile communications industry association GSMA. Mobile penetration in Kenya is well over 70%.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Through a simple text message, MFarm allows rural farmers in remote areas of Kenya to check the latest market prices, post information on their harvest for buyers to see and purchase, and band together with other farmers in their area to make bulk purchases of expensive but needed agricultural inputs like seeds, fertilisers and equipment at discounted rates. It also allows them to join together to sell their harvests, making it easier for buyers to reach them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;MFarm&rsquo;s approach hopes to improve the agricultural sector by in a sense leapfrogging physical infrastructure troubles, and using existing mobile communications infrastructure to increase buyer/seller relationships and transactions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Before [services like MFarm], farmers were being exploited by middlemen,&rdquo; claims Adrian Mukhebi, Chairman of the Kenya Agricultural Commodities Exchange (KACE). &ldquo;Now, with information via mobiles, farmers are better able to bargain prices against middlemen, and can in some cases increase 25-35% of their profits.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Technology&rsquo;s role in improving access for farmers may be exciting and hold great potential, Mukhebi claims, but as these tools and information are not yet available to all farmers it still has some way to go. But if and when they do, perhaps Africa could begin fulfilling its vast potential &ndash; feeding itself and the rest of the world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-04-08T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Tech fix for Africas big farming challenge","HeadlineShort":"Tech taps Africas farm potential","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Africa could help feed the world if its farmland was properly utilised.
