e","Slug":"20130903-boston-restoring-its-tech-cred"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"b432f046-f6be-4938-8f28-d03e19af05e7","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130903-boston-restoring-its-tech-cred","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130903-boston-restoring-its-tech-cred"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130903-boston-restoring-its-tech-cred","_id":"559a5e0e157e584f77e20663"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[{"Image":[],"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"gallery","Guid":"","Id":"","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:gallery:p01ft98p","_id":"559a5ab5157e584f77e17fbe"}],"AssetImage":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":436696,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1f/tc/p01ftcz9.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Skolkovo in winter (copyright: Saltans/Jaeger and Partner)","SynopsisMedium":"Skolkovo in winter (copyright: Saltans/Jaeger and Partner)","SynopsisShort":"Skolkovo in winter (copyright: Saltans/Jaeger and Partner)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1f/tc/p01ftcz9.jpg","Title":"skolkovo_winter_976.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01ftcz9","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01ftcz9","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01ftcz9","_id":"559a5c42157e584f77e1b893"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":null,"Description":"","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Dmitry Saltykovsky","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"912566d1-6a6e-4da1-889f-af8c045841e7","Id":"wwfuture/author/dmitry-saltykovsky","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"dmitry-saltykovsky"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/dmitry-saltykovsky","_id":"559a5a52157e584f77e17111"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Sitting on 400 hectares of the most expensive Russian real estate outside central Moscow, Skolkovo is intended to be one of the biggest tech innovation centres in the world. In 2010, then-president Dmitry Medvedev gave orders to create an innovative centre from scratch &ndash; this in a country where all tech parks and scientific centres have been inherited from the Soviet past. &ldquo;We have money but don&rsquo;t have our Silicon Valley,&rdquo; he said on his visit to Silicon Valley, after earlier stressing it was a project &ldquo;that should become the largest test ground for Russian new economic policy.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But at the moment plans for an internationally renowned tech hub a half-hour&rsquo;s drive from Red Square are still a dream. The fantasy starts with the idea that it takes half an hour to drive there through Moscow&rsquo;s notoriously heavy traffic; on a weekday it takes a few hours. When you reach there you are greeted with signs saying &ldquo;Skolkovo - we are making the future today&rdquo;, but beyond the perimeter fence lies a couple of cranes and an excavator digging the foundations of this future fantasy. Several hundred metres away stands the only finished building, called Hypercube; it is supposed to be an example of the eco-friendly principles intended for Skolkovo. Looking at the building from the dusty and polluted highway, however, makes you wonder just how green the enterprise will truly be.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Three years after its announcement by President Medvedev, Skolkovo still raises more questions than answers. Getting Skolkovo off the ground has not been cheap, with billions of oil dollars spent, and yet more to be allocated. There are doubts that the Russian government &ndash; now headed by Vladimir Putin &ndash; has the conviction to keep going. The investments are impressive though. The road to be built to Skolkovo and around the area is said to cost $2 billion. However, the biggest questions facing start-ups that have, or want to, set up shop there is where they are going to work and live. The whole site is supposed to be finished by 2020.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One source close to Skolkovo said that the project is modeled on tech centres such as &lt;a href=\"http://www.sophia-antipolis.org/\"&gt;Sophia Antipolis&lt;/a&gt; in France, &lt;a href=\"http://www.cyberjaya-msc.com/\"&gt;Cyberjaya&lt;/a&gt; in Malaysia or &lt;a href=\"http://www.masdar.ae/en/\"&gt;Masdar&lt;/a&gt; in UAE. While the start-ups wait for the so needed infrastructure to be finished in 2015-16, they are being sent to other scientific centres to carry on their projects. Skolkovo&rsquo;s vice-president for external communications and advertising, Alexander Chernov, has tried to assure the press everything is fine and will be finished soon. &ldquo;It is a usual trend with construction business in Russia. Two years shift is not that bad but we will welcome our residents in 2015. If not, we will look like losers.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Russia has a long history of scientific and technological breakthroughs, but it is suffering from a hangover from recent decades. The Cold War and the Iron Curtain played a key role in maintaining a competitive and innovative spirit in closed science cities of the Soviet Union, such as &lt;a href=\"http://novosibirskguide.com/Akademgorodok/\"&gt;Akademgorodok&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Core disciplines&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But after the Cold War, everything changed. The new Russia found itself with the remnants of the once powerful Soviet scientific machine and a huge brain drain in the direction of the US. Innovative ideas started to matter very little in a new capitalist society focused on selling Russia&rsquo;s vast natural resources and cashing in US dollars. It took two decades for a project like Skolkovo to emerge from the Kremlin&rsquo;s corridors.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what will Skolkovo concentrate on? There are five core areas: information technology, nuclear technology, energy efficiency, biomedical innovation, and space and telecommunications. Companies receiving resident status can work on their projects and attempt to bring them to market. Skolkovo&rsquo;s grants and construction are mostly backed up by the money from the Russian government. The investment is supposed to reach over $15bn (&pound;10bn) by 2020. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the past couple of years, Skolkovo has received over 10,000 online applications. Applicants range from companies and startup groups to scientific centres, universities, student groups and distinguished scientists. Most applicants fail to become residents. &ldquo;The main challenge here is either a project doesn&rsquo;t fit into the five clusters or it hasn&rsquo;t been worked through. Sometimes companies overestimate their abilities or their idea has already been developed in the market,&rdquo; says Chernov.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Currently, more than 900 Skolkovo residents or startup companies are working on projects across the five clusters. Many of them have already received grants from the Skolkovo Fund said to expect to be worth $2bn (&pound;1.28bn) by 2019.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the grant recipients is &lt;a href=\"http://www.fruct.org/\"&gt;Fruct&lt;/a&gt; founded by Russian and Finnish universities together with Nokia. Although they won the money last year, they should receive the $300,000 (&pound;191,000) grant this autumn to develop a mobile device which monitors eight key diagnostic markers such as cardiovascular, pulmonology and endocrinologic readings, to help monitor potential health problems such as arterial hypertension arrhythmia, asthma, and &nbsp;diabetes.. Asked about how they will spend the money, the head of Fruct, Sergey Balandin, says: &ldquo;The challenge was to make investors believe in our project. We will put more emphasis on the research now. We will try to improve the weak sides of our project with the help of Skolkovo Fund&rsquo;s specialists and prepare the product to enter the market.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Other hopefuls have not been so lucky. &lt;a href=\"http://www.bravomotors.ru/\"&gt;Bravo Motors&lt;/a&gt; applied for the grant to finance its project for an ultra-light electric vehicle called the e-Trike. The main challenge for Bravo now is to find $1.4 m (&pound;890,000) in investment to complete the development of the e-Trike, carry out bench and field testing, and start production. But to get tax incentives, the company had to change the e-Trike&rsquo;s colour from red to green &ndash; the same colour used on Skolkovo&rsquo;s logo. Being part of Skolkovo may mean compromises.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Distrustful politicians &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Skolkovo has not been without rumours of corruption, either. Medvedev tried to avoid corruption by appointing Russian oligarch Victor Vekselberg as the President of Skolkovo. But 2013 started with several cases. &lt;a href=\"http://www.skoltech.ru/\"&gt;Skoltech University&lt;/a&gt; is being created to educate in-house innovators of tomorrow. MIT is heavily involved with the project, helping Skoltech with the structure, educational and research programs. The university hasn&rsquo;t been built yet &ndash; but already it has created a corruption scandal. The state audit office found out recently that millions of dollars allocated to Skolkovo&rsquo;s Skoltech, went, instead, to finance programs and grants at MIT. The government tried to bury the story, but &lt;a href=\"http://izvestia.ru/news/548178\"&gt;Russian newspaper Izvestia&lt;/a&gt; got hold of the case documents&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then there is the political situation to contend with. Even Skolkovo&rsquo;s biggest cheerleaders understand start-ups like the e-Trike won&rsquo;t be able to make a return on the multi-billion-dollar investments poured into the project. Russian politicians, in general, are distrustful of innovations they don&rsquo;t understand such as a recent discussion at Skolkovo on the creation perpetuum mobile.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A source close to Skolkovo who wished to remain anonymous, says: &nbsp;&ldquo;The success of Skolkovo should be assessed on the basis of whether it manages to create a huge and successful business like Yandex [Russia&rsquo;s biggest search engine] and Mail.ru [Russian email].&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The challenge for Skolkovo is to prove it can make money, rather than just spend it. It has been created from the very top of the political spectrum - with huge investments &ndash; but the buildings remain unconstructed, and the ideas that could make it profitable remain untapped.&nbsp; The question is, will this always be the case?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-28T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Skolkovo: Tech city that aims to restore nation's pride","HeadlineShort":"Russia: Restoring its tech pride?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In the Soviet era, Russian science was lavished with money and resources. But post-communism, innovation has slumped. Can a new tech city reverse its fortunes?\n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Japanese start-up entrepreneur Masayoshi Hashimoto believes he knows where the country&rsquo;s IT industry is getting its guidance from; the Czech surrealist author and satirist Franz Kafka.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Working for a Japanese web services firm, I couldn&rsquo;t believe how badly things were done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;We had everyone managing projects using Excel. Excel, I ask you! It beggars belief. But managers here like the old ways.&rdquo; When changes were suggested he was stonewalled.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Venting his irritation on Twitter led him to a conversation about the business practices afflicting Japan&rsquo;s tech sector with a fellow malcontent. One face-to-face meeting later, he quit his job to form a company with his new comrade rebel with and created the website &lt;a href=\"http://www.paradiseware.net\"&gt;Paradiseware&lt;/a&gt;, which &lt;a href=\"https://app.mmth.tw/?lang=en\"&gt;helps businesses manage projects more efficiently&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The result is typically Japanese; run on a shoestring without any of the benefits Silicon Valley start-ups might attract.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan has a real problem with innovation. It&rsquo;s just not very Japanese. Nor, you might be surprised to read, is originality. It&rsquo;s the kind of thing swaggering foreigners or suspect loners are thought to meddle in. So if you visit Japan and find ersatz statues of Liberty in tourist spots, or a very &lt;a href=\"http://www.cooljapan.org/\"&gt;uncool bureaucratic drive&lt;/a&gt; to promote Japanese creativity and culture ripped off from Cool Britannia - you&rsquo;ll begin to understand why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan is \"good at technology but poor in business\" as one official puts it. &ldquo;Its high levels of basic research and superior technology are hampered by too-strict regulations, and vertically integrated administration. The gap between researchers and manufacturers, in many cases, prevents brilliant R&amp;D results from being put into practical use - the &lsquo;Death Valley&rsquo; problem.&rdquo; Japan leads in its high number of early adopters. Thanks to them, inventors can gain confidence and the initial momentum needed to push off a new idea. But it often doesn&rsquo;t last long.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Unfortunately this is accompanied by a very envious, critical and vocal audience. They underline every possible reason that the product will fail and keep doing so indefatigably. They attack the innovator on Twitter, Facebook, blogs, etc; and they keep doing so until the innovator raises a white flag.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Add to this 20 year&rsquo;s economic stagnation, zero appetite for risk and eroded confidence and Japan emerges as one the toughest environments on Earth to root new ideas.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Struggling start-ups&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are backers in Japan, his partner Shuku Tanizaki explains, but most are interested only in no-risk ventures. Start-ups have to attract crowd-funding or, as is more usual, the personal savings of their creators. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s lots of venture capitalists here but they are only interested in games and new media,&rdquo; says Hashimoto. Even renting conventional office space can be troublesome in Japan - landlords like to see two years&rsquo; profits before they open the door. Paradiseware operates out of cheap, shared office space in central Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think starting a new business means also confronting the problems afflicting your society,&rdquo; says Mr Hashimoto.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite being perceived as a big-hitting innovator, Japan has a tiny, struggling start-up landscape that has yet to make an impact abroad. &ldquo;This is because this sector is the most under-supported in Japan,&rdquo; says Terrie Lloyd, an Australian entrepreneur and commentator based in Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As the global digital economy grows in importance, this puts Japan, the world&rsquo;s third-biggest economy and one-time electronics giant, at a massive disadvantage. America and even tiny South Korea continue to come up with the gadgets and smartphone-based services that consumers want. Japan&rsquo;s sluggish megacorporations such as Sony are unable to match the swift-footed foreign competition and fall further and further behind.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What strangles originality here, explains Tokyo-based technology consultant Nobuyuki Hayashi, is that Japanese innovation keeps to very rigid structures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Most of them went to the same university, studied the same subjects and never met people from outside the group. This is because of the Japanese educational system and how Japanese recruiting is done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Japanese companies tend to hire graduates from the same school.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This spectacular failure to respond to the internet age is slowly being challenged, however, both by the country&rsquo;s new government and a few entrepreneurs. Copying ideas from Silicon Valley, top entrepreneurs and officials have tried for over a decade now to spark some life into Japan&rsquo;s hi-tech sector by increasing incubation projects and the like. Japan now has over 400 business incubators up from 30 in 1999 some of which are focused on the hi-tech sector. Most offer funding, work space and mentoring.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And after being held back by the entropy of their elders for so long, Japan&rsquo;s enterprising young are going it alone. An experienced user interface designer in Japan and abroad, 26-year-old Alexander Takahashi simply rents shared office space, and networks for Angel investors when he can, to develop a location-based service named &lt;a href=\"https://itunes.apple.com/us/app/rooftops/id552140477?mt=8\"&gt;Rooftops&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He understands the reservations. &ldquo;Japanese businessmen tend not to trust the younger generation. No matter how well you present yourself - young equals bad,&rdquo; says Takahashi.&nbsp; &ldquo;And honestly I can understand them. There are no really new start-ups with new ideas nowadays.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japanese young entrepreneurs are enthusiastic but lack crucial business know-how, says Lloyd. &ldquo;They are totally unprepared for the business world, and this is why cloistered accelerators and incubators are so popular here,&rdquo; he adds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hitoshi Masuda, who until recently headed up the government&rsquo;s small-business think tank, disagrees. He says: &ldquo;Japan is changing profoundly but dynamically in terms of who drives innovation and how innovation is being driven.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Economic reforms under a new government - Abenomics, named for the PM Shinzo Abe - are having some positive effects. His party has also promised to fight the corner of all innovators, risk takers and creative types.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their model is a bunch of maverick, by Japanese standards, entrepreneurs which include Japan&rsquo;s third-richest man, Masayoshi Son, born to a Korean immigrant family, whose mobile phone empire Softbank is funding a brave attempt at commercialising home-grown green tech innovations. His outsider outlook was a huge benefit, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;If you look at successful Japanese start-ups, they are mostly run by CEOs who have applied the &lsquo;foreign connection&rsquo; in some way - be it education, money, technology, business deals, or talent,&rdquo; says Lloyd. &ldquo;In some cases, like Mr Son, he has managed to combine all five factors.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those factors seem to have inoculated such champions against fear of failure making them bold at the same time. They represent a new way of being Japanese &ndash; one the nation urgently needs to get to grips with.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-21T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Can Japan reboot its anti-innovation start-up culture?","HeadlineShort":"Can Japan plug its start-up gap?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Japan has long been hailed as one of the worlds most developed nations. But when it comes to innovation, things are less rosy. Why is it stifling its start-ups?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"Japan has long been hailed as one of the worlds most developed nations. But its start-up culture is falling behind. How can it bounce back?","SummaryShort":"Tackling the countrys lack of innovation","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"c9500398-ed51-4dc1-8694-e64d1f783cc6","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130820-plugging-japans-start-up-gap","_id":"559a5e0d157e584f77e2063b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When new businesses based on the computer chip began to cluster in Santa Clara Valley, it did not take long for someone to come up with the name Silicon Valley. That set a pattern, and soon there was &lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/business/3249891.stm\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Glen&lt;/a&gt; in Scotland, Kenya&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-21173158\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Savannah&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://www.silicongulf.net/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Gulf&lt;/a&gt; in the Philippines, &lt;a href=\"http://www.academpark.com/en/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Taiga&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia &ndash; even the somewhat less romantic-sounding &lt;a href=\"http://www.siliconroundabout.org.uk/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Roundabout&lt;/a&gt; in London.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now there is Silicon Allee in Berlin (Allee, meaning alley or avenue in German, is part of many street names in the city), which to some observers evokes the atmosphere of the early days in Northern California. That was certainly the vibe at &lt;a href=\"http://toaberlin.com/\"&gt;Tech Open Air Berlin&lt;/a&gt;, a gathering held earlier this month in a ramshackle complex of wooden and stone buildings in Kreuzberg, a trendy part of the city. There was soft acoustic music and falafel, and a lot of beautiful people exuding a studied coolness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to see a lot of amazing stuff coming out of Berlin,&rdquo; says Tikhon Bernstam, who came over from Silicon Valley to attend the event. &ldquo;This sort of culture that you see here, the hippy music, we had exactly the same thing in San Francisco a few years ago. I&rsquo;ve been impressed by the quality of the companies I&rsquo;ve been seeing here.&rdquo; Bernstam&rsquo;s cloud data service he co-founded, Parse, was recently bought by Facebook for $85 million.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, Berlin is many leagues behind Silicon Valley. From Adobe through to Yahoo, via Apple, Intel, Google and Oracle, the Valley, as its occupants call it, has a combined wealth which most countries would envy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exact figures are hard to come by, but Berlin came ninth in &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticcities.com/jobs-and-economy/2013/06/new-global-start-cities/5144/\"&gt;a recent global ranking&lt;/a&gt; of cities with the most tech start-ups founded since 2002 that are global, or received angel funding or institutional venture capital. The top three spots were occupied by San Francisco-Silicon Valley with 201 start-ups, New York (144) and London (90). Berlin had 27, just ahead of Bangalore on 26 and Sao Paulo on 21.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But with its reputation for doing things differently, very cheap rents, and the ambition and optimism on show at events like Tech Open Air Berlin, the hope is that its scene will deliver on the buzz that has surrounded it for the past few years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The city itself is in many ways a start up. From 1945 it was shattered and split for almost half a century. The eastern, Soviet-controlled zone was hardly a seed-bed for innovation. Industry moved out from the West. Students were exempted from military service, attracting a young, artistic and politically radical alternative scene.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the fall of the Wall in 1989, the city has been re-inventing itself. The vibrant alternative culture remains in place, and some see it as providing fertile ground for the creation of disruptive technologies &ndash; though others argue that a counterculture-led antipathy to money-making may hamper innovation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One thing that is clear is that property is cheap &ndash; Berlin still has rents which are so low that they make visitors from London and New York gasp. The average annual cost of a workstation in an office in Berlin, including maintenance costs, taxes and rent, was $8,410 at the end of last year, compared with $14,050 for New York and $14,620 in the City of London, according to &lt;a href=\"http://dtz.b15media.com/downloads/DTZ_Occupier_Perspective_Global_Occupancy_Costs_Offices_2013.pdf\"&gt;a report&lt;/a&gt; published by the global property company DTZ. Renting a 120 square metre apartment in Berlin costs around a third what it does in London and New York.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This combination has attracted many who aspire to be the next big thing in tech. In a few cases they have already succeeded, such as the social game developer &lt;a href=\"http://www.wooga.com/\"&gt;Wooga&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/\"&gt;Research Gate&lt;/a&gt;, a networking website for scientists. The darling of the scene is &lt;a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/\"&gt;SoundCloud&lt;/a&gt;, an audio sharing platform that has 200 million users every month. Alex Ljung, its co-founder and chief executive, moved the company from Stockholm to Berlin because he felt it offered a richer mix of people involved in music, and because of the ample and cheap office space.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, Ljung admits his firm&rsquo;s new hometown doesn&rsquo;t cover all bases. &ldquo;With start-up life, there&rsquo;s never really much calmness. It&rsquo;s all jet and no lag,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;In an average month, I spend about a week-and-a-half in Berlin, about a week in San Francisco and the rest of the time split between Los Angeles, New York and London.&rdquo; What Berlin lacks that San Francisco has is the sheer volume of people who&rsquo;ve grown tech companies, where Ljung says he can bump into others who have trodden the same path, and who can advise him on short-cuts and pitfalls. In New York, London and Los Angeles, he can meet plenty of people with money to invest in good ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Bernstam, investors from the US are scared to invest in Germany because they don&rsquo;t understand the way companies are structured and corporate regulations. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t understand what could happen if future investors tried to dilute us,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;and investors from the States don&rsquo;t understand yet how that works abroad &ndash; and are frankly scared of investing abroad.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All of which created a chicken-and-egg problem in Berlin. Some entrepreneurs have stayed away because of the lack of investors, and those with the money have preferred to go elsewhere because of the greater number of companies to invest in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this resistance is beginning breaking down, on both sides, according to David Knight, editor-in-chief of Berlin tech scene blog &lt;a href=\"http://siliconallee.com/\"&gt;Silicon Allee&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s been this streak of creativity and once you got a core of people, which started round 2009, you started to get a lot of investors from the US and Europe looking to Berlin. It&rsquo;s still at a very early stage, but there&rsquo;s a lot more money now than there was two years ago.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Districts such as Kreuzberg, Neukoelln and Prenzlauer Berg, where would-be entrepreneurs congregate in the bars and cafes offering wi-fi, are now seeing the effect of this. In Prenzlauer Berg, &lt;a href=\"http://www.factoryberlin.com/\"&gt;Factory&lt;/a&gt;, which received &euro;1 million in funding from Google, has rented space to over 15 companies, including SoundCloud. The city also now has a lot more of what might be called serious business people. Florian Lanzer is trying to build a website selling green products to people who are not necessarily committed environmentalists. He said the project has attracted a lot of interest, including the involvement of a former CEO of Sony Germany.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;What Berlin gets right is that it attracts the right kind of people,&rdquo; says Lanzer. &ldquo;Young people with talent and people with long track records and massive experience are coming together.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around a decade ago, Berlin was famously described as being &ldquo;poor, but sexy&rdquo; by its mayor, Klaus Wowereit. The hope is that creativity and commerce is now beginning to combine in tech in such a way that it might finally shed its poor tag.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Berlin start-ups: Cool and commerce coming together?","HeadlineShort":"Berlin: Can cool and capital mix?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In a new series Innovation Cultures takes an on-the-ground look at tech scenes around the world that hope in some way to emulate the successes of Silicon Valley. First up is Berlin, where Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"In the first of a new series looking at global tech scenes, Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","SummaryShort":"Hip start-up city may be coming of age","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"4b0f1c8f-2f5c-472d-8d83-5265b87745f1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","_id":"559a5e0c157e584f77e2061d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s three in the afternoon, and in their Nasa lab in Silicon Valley, California, two engineers are playing with a toy designed for toddlers. The melon-sized plaything consists of a tactile lattice of brightly painted beads, connected by wooden rods and elastic cords. It twists and flexes as Vytas Sunspiral and Adrian Agogino crunch it in their hands and throw it between themselves across the room.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One online review describes the gadget as &ldquo;great for sensory exploration,&rdquo; but Sunspiral and Agogino are considering it for something way more ambitious: planetary exploration. This bundle of beads, rods and cords, they believe, could form the basis of a new generation of planetary rovers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The programme that funded this bit of research, I call it the crazy ideas programme,&rdquo; exclaims the fast-talking Sunspiral, a towering figure whose name, long blonde hair, beard and glasses suggest a &ldquo;crazy ideas&rdquo; lab in Silicon Valley is his natural environment. For obvious reasons, Nasa prefers not to use the word &ldquo;crazy&rdquo; in its research but includes the project under its &lt;a href=\"http://www.nasa.gov/offices/oct/stp/niac/index.html\"&gt;Innovative Advanced Concepts Program&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;Here, in the intelligent robotics group,&rdquo; says Sunspiral, &ldquo;we do all sorts of advanced research on robots.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The toy Sunspiral and Agogino are playing with uses what is technically known as a tensegrity system. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a system where, unlike our buildings where everything&rsquo;s held together rigidly, everything&rsquo;s held together in tension,&rdquo; Sunspiral explains. &ldquo;So you end up with a network of cables that hold rods. You will often have seen artwork that looks like this &ndash; kind of crazy weird bars, floating in space.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The components are very simple,&rdquo; adds Agogino, twisting the toy in his hands as he speaks. &ldquo;They&rsquo;re just rods and cables connected together, and rods never connect to rods and cables never connect to cables. A child can hit themselves over the head with it and they won&rsquo;t get hurt. That happens to be great for what we want.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To prove the point, Vytas encourages me to throw the toy on the floor. Hard. It slams into the floor, squashes together a bit and bounces. &ldquo;Think of what&rsquo;s happening when it bounces,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s absorbing energy when it&rsquo;s going down and it&rsquo;s releasing energy when it&rsquo;s going up. It doesn&rsquo;t break because the energy is distributed.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Garage games&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;You can imagine that dropping one of these on the surface of an alien world would certainly be a lot easier than landing the existing types of robotic rovers with all their precision engineered wheels, motors and instruments. Past missions, such as &lt;a href=\"http://marsrovers.jpl.nasa.gov/home/index.html\"&gt;the Spirit and Opportunity rovers&lt;/a&gt;, used airbags to cushion their landings. Because of its hefty size, Nasa&rsquo;s Curiosity had to be lowered on cables &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120719-how-to-land-on-mars\"&gt;using its elaborate sky-crane system&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;Drop one of those rovers 30 feet,&rdquo; says Agogino, &ldquo;do you think it&rsquo;s going to be very happy about it?&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But existing planetary rovers are shaped the way they are for a reason: they need wheels to move around, bodies to hold equipment and arms to deploy instruments or cameras. So how do you do that with a glorified bundle of beads, rods and elastic? &ldquo;We get a lot of our inspiration from biological systems,&rdquo; says Sunspiral, who suggests a mechanism that mimics how our own muscles expand and contract could offer one solution. &ldquo;You could shorten and lengthen the cables to cause the whole thing to roll and move around.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The instruments would be suspended within the centre of the structure to protect them from damage &ndash; cushioned from the impact of landing and locomotion. But they would have to be very different to the sort of boxy equipment carried by existing rovers, capable of being tumbled around and with any arms or probes, safely tucked away. There could even be several of these rovers, tumbling across an alien world and communicating with each other like a swarm of bees.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The rover Sunspiral and Agogino are currently putting their minds to is Super Ball Bot, which they are developing as a concept mission to explore Saturn&rsquo;s moon Titan. With its petrochemical atmosphere and hydrocarbon lakes, Titan is a fascinating destination and one of the potential candidates in the Solar System for primitive life. The &lt;a href=\"http://sci.esa.int/science-e/www/object/index.cfm?fobjectid=45751\"&gt;European Space Agency&rsquo;s Huygens probe&lt;/a&gt; parachuted down onto Titan&rsquo;s surface in 2005 but the cookie-shaped spacecraft had no ability to move around and only managed to return data for around 90 minutes in the hostile environment.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Something like Super Ball Bot would be ideal to survive a descent through the thick atmosphere and cope with movement on the moon&rsquo;s surface &ndash; which includes solid ground, slush and sea. &ldquo;Titan is one of the most interesting moons,&rdquo; says Agogino. &ldquo;We can&rsquo;t see the surface [from space] and the atmosphere is full of clouds. We really need something robust, or five or six things that are robust, to get the science done.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So will any of this actually happen or is it just another &ldquo;crazy&rdquo; idea?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Absolutely,&rdquo; insists Sunspiral. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been around the robotics industry long enough to know that you just have to throw yourself at a vision and make progress and be successful and anything&rsquo;s possible. It&rsquo;s just like Silicon Valley and all the start-ups here. You&rsquo;ve got to start with a couple of kids in a garage and eventually you have your Google.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And, I suggest, they are both like those kids in the garage? &ldquo;Exactly,&rdquo; says Agogino as they go back to playing with their toy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt; page or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","HeadlineLong":"Nasas crazy robot lab","HeadlineShort":"Nasas crazy robot lab","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Inside a laboratory in California, space engineers are designing a new generation of rover... and they look like nothing you have seen before. start-up entrepreneur Masayoshi Hashimoto believes he knows where the country&rsquo;s IT industry is getting its guidance from; the Czech surrealist author and satirist Franz Kafka.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Working for a Japanese web services firm, I couldn&rsquo;t believe how badly things were done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;We had everyone managing projects using Excel. Excel, I ask you! It beggars belief. But managers here like the old ways.&rdquo; When changes were suggested he was stonewalled.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Venting his irritation on Twitter led him to a conversation about the business practices afflicting Japan&rsquo;s tech sector with a fellow malcontent. One face-to-face meeting later, he quit his job to form a company with his new comrade rebel with and created the website &lt;a href=\"http://www.paradiseware.net\"&gt;Paradiseware&lt;/a&gt;, which &lt;a href=\"https://app.mmth.tw/?lang=en\"&gt;helps businesses manage projects more efficiently&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The result is typically Japanese; run on a shoestring without any of the benefits Silicon Valley start-ups might attract.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan has a real problem with innovation. It&rsquo;s just not very Japanese. Nor, you might be surprised to read, is originality. It&rsquo;s the kind of thing swaggering foreigners or suspect loners are thought to meddle in. So if you visit Japan and find ersatz statues of Liberty in tourist spots, or a very &lt;a href=\"http://www.cooljapan.org/\"&gt;uncool bureaucratic drive&lt;/a&gt; to promote Japanese creativity and culture ripped off from Cool Britannia - you&rsquo;ll begin to understand why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japan is \"good at technology but poor in business\" as one official puts it. &ldquo;Its high levels of basic research and superior technology are hampered by too-strict regulations, and vertically integrated administration. The gap between researchers and manufacturers, in many cases, prevents brilliant R&amp;D results from being put into practical use - the &lsquo;Death Valley&rsquo; problem.&rdquo; Japan leads in its high number of early adopters. Thanks to them, inventors can gain confidence and the initial momentum needed to push off a new idea. But it often doesn&rsquo;t last long.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Unfortunately this is accompanied by a very envious, critical and vocal audience. They underline every possible reason that the product will fail and keep doing so indefatigably. They attack the innovator on Twitter, Facebook, blogs, etc; and they keep doing so until the innovator raises a white flag.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Add to this 20 year&rsquo;s economic stagnation, zero appetite for risk and eroded confidence and Japan emerges as one the toughest environments on Earth to root new ideas.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Struggling start-ups&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are backers in Japan, his partner Shuku Tanizaki explains, but most are interested only in no-risk ventures. Start-ups have to attract crowd-funding or, as is more usual, the personal savings of their creators. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s lots of venture capitalists here but they are only interested in games and new media,&rdquo; says Hashimoto. Even renting conventional office space can be troublesome in Japan - landlords like to see two years&rsquo; profits before they open the door. Paradiseware operates out of cheap, shared office space in central Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I think starting a new business means also confronting the problems afflicting your society,&rdquo; says Mr Hashimoto.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite being perceived as a big-hitting innovator, Japan has a tiny, struggling start-up landscape that has yet to make an impact abroad. &ldquo;This is because this sector is the most under-supported in Japan,&rdquo; says Terrie Lloyd, an Australian entrepreneur and commentator based in Tokyo.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As the global digital economy grows in importance, this puts Japan, the world&rsquo;s third-biggest economy and one-time electronics giant, at a massive disadvantage. America and even tiny South Korea continue to come up with the gadgets and smartphone-based services that consumers want. Japan&rsquo;s sluggish megacorporations such as Sony are unable to match the swift-footed foreign competition and fall further and further behind.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What strangles originality here, explains Tokyo-based technology consultant Nobuyuki Hayashi, is that Japanese innovation keeps to very rigid structures.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Most of them went to the same university, studied the same subjects and never met people from outside the group. This is because of the Japanese educational system and how Japanese recruiting is done,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Japanese companies tend to hire graduates from the same school.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This spectacular failure to respond to the internet age is slowly being challenged, however, both by the country&rsquo;s new government and a few entrepreneurs. Copying ideas from Silicon Valley, top entrepreneurs and officials have tried for over a decade now to spark some life into Japan&rsquo;s hi-tech sector by increasing incubation projects and the like. Japan now has over 400 business incubators up from 30 in 1999 some of which are focused on the hi-tech sector. Most offer funding, work space and mentoring.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And after being held back by the entropy of their elders for so long, Japan&rsquo;s enterprising young are going it alone. An experienced user interface designer in Japan and abroad, 26-year-old Alexander Takahashi simply rents shared office space, and networks for Angel investors when he can, to develop a location-based service named &lt;a href=\"https://itunes.apple.com/us/app/rooftops/id552140477?mt=8\"&gt;Rooftops&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He understands the reservations. &ldquo;Japanese businessmen tend not to trust the younger generation. No matter how well you present yourself - young equals bad,&rdquo; says Takahashi.&nbsp; &ldquo;And honestly I can understand them. There are no really new start-ups with new ideas nowadays.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Japanese young entrepreneurs are enthusiastic but lack crucial business know-how, says Lloyd. &ldquo;They are totally unprepared for the business world, and this is why cloistered accelerators and incubators are so popular here,&rdquo; he adds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hitoshi Masuda, who until recently headed up the government&rsquo;s small-business think tank, disagrees. He says: &ldquo;Japan is changing profoundly but dynamically in terms of who drives innovation and how innovation is being driven.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Economic reforms under a new government - Abenomics, named for the PM Shinzo Abe - are having some positive effects. His party has also promised to fight the corner of all innovators, risk takers and creative types.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Their model is a bunch of maverick, by Japanese standards, entrepreneurs which include Japan&rsquo;s third-richest man, Masayoshi Son, born to a Korean immigrant family, whose mobile phone empire Softbank is funding a brave attempt at commercialising home-grown green tech innovations. His outsider outlook was a huge benefit, he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;If you look at successful Japanese start-ups, they are mostly run by CEOs who have applied the &lsquo;foreign connection&rsquo; in some way - be it education, money, technology, business deals, or talent,&rdquo; says Lloyd. &ldquo;In some cases, like Mr Son, he has managed to combine all five factors.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Those factors seem to have inoculated such champions against fear of failure making them bold at the same time. They represent a new way of being Japanese &ndash; one the nation urgently needs to get to grips with.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-21T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Can Japan reboot its anti-innovation start-up culture?","HeadlineShort":"Can Japan plug its start-up gap?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Japan has long been hailed as one of the worlds most developed nations. But when it comes to innovation, things are less rosy. Why is it stifling its start-ups?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When new businesses based on the computer chip began to cluster in Santa Clara Valley, it did not take long for someone to come up with the name Silicon Valley. That set a pattern, and soon there was &lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/business/3249891.stm\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Glen&lt;/a&gt; in Scotland, Kenya&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-21173158\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Savannah&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://www.silicongulf.net/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Gulf&lt;/a&gt; in the Philippines, &lt;a href=\"http://www.academpark.com/en/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Taiga&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia &ndash; even the somewhat less romantic-sounding &lt;a href=\"http://www.siliconroundabout.org.uk/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Roundabout&lt;/a&gt; in London.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now there is Silicon Allee in Berlin (Allee, meaning alley or avenue in German, is part of many street names in the city), which to some observers evokes the atmosphere of the early days in Northern California. That was certainly the vibe at &lt;a href=\"http://toaberlin.com/\"&gt;Tech Open Air Berlin&lt;/a&gt;, a gathering held earlier this month in a ramshackle complex of wooden and stone buildings in Kreuzberg, a trendy part of the city. There was soft acoustic music and falafel, and a lot of beautiful people exuding a studied coolness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to see a lot of amazing stuff coming out of Berlin,&rdquo; says Tikhon Bernstam, who came over from Silicon Valley to attend the event. &ldquo;This sort of culture that you see here, the hippy music, we had exactly the same thing in San Francisco a few years ago. I&rsquo;ve been impressed by the quality of the companies I&rsquo;ve been seeing here.&rdquo; Bernstam&rsquo;s cloud data service he co-founded, Parse, was recently bought by Facebook for $85 million.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, Berlin is many leagues behind Silicon Valley. From Adobe through to Yahoo, via Apple, Intel, Google and Oracle, the Valley, as its occupants call it, has a combined wealth which most countries would envy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exact figures are hard to come by, but Berlin came ninth in &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticcities.com/jobs-and-economy/2013/06/new-global-start-cities/5144/\"&gt;a recent global ranking&lt;/a&gt; of cities with the most tech start-ups founded since 2002 that are global, or received angel funding or institutional venture capital. The top three spots were occupied by San Francisco-Silicon Valley with 201 start-ups, New York (144) and London (90). Berlin had 27, just ahead of Bangalore on 26 and Sao Paulo on 21.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But with its reputation for doing things differently, very cheap rents, and the ambition and optimism on show at events like Tech Open Air Berlin, the hope is that its scene will deliver on the buzz that has surrounded it for the past few years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The city itself is in many ways a start up. From 1945 it was shattered and split for almost half a century. The eastern, Soviet-controlled zone was hardly a seed-bed for innovation. Industry moved out from the West. Students were exempted from military service, attracting a young, artistic and politically radical alternative scene.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the fall of the Wall in 1989, the city has been re-inventing itself. The vibrant alternative culture remains in place, and some see it as providing fertile ground for the creation of disruptive technologies &ndash; though others argue that a counterculture-led antipathy to money-making may hamper innovation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One thing that is clear is that property is cheap &ndash; Berlin still has rents which are so low that they make visitors from London and New York gasp. The average annual cost of a workstation in an office in Berlin, including maintenance costs, taxes and rent, was $8,410 at the end of last year, compared with $14,050 for New York and $14,620 in the City of London, according to &lt;a href=\"http://dtz.b15media.com/downloads/DTZ_Occupier_Perspective_Global_Occupancy_Costs_Offices_2013.pdf\"&gt;a report&lt;/a&gt; published by the global property company DTZ. Renting a 120 square metre apartment in Berlin costs around a third what it does in London and New York.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This combination has attracted many who aspire to be the next big thing in tech. In a few cases they have already succeeded, such as the social game developer &lt;a href=\"http://www.wooga.com/\"&gt;Wooga&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/\"&gt;Research Gate&lt;/a&gt;, a networking website for scientists. The darling of the scene is &lt;a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/\"&gt;SoundCloud&lt;/a&gt;, an audio sharing platform that has 200 million users every month. Alex Ljung, its co-founder and chief executive, moved the company from Stockholm to Berlin because he felt it offered a richer mix of people involved in music, and because of the ample and cheap office space.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, Ljung admits his firm&rsquo;s new hometown doesn&rsquo;t cover all bases. &ldquo;With start-up life, there&rsquo;s never really much calmness. It&rsquo;s all jet and no lag,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;In an average month, I spend about a week-and-a-half in Berlin, about a week in San Francisco and the rest of the time split between Los Angeles, New York and London.&rdquo; What Berlin lacks that San Francisco has is the sheer volume of people who&rsquo;ve grown tech companies, where Ljung says he can bump into others who have trodden the same path, and who can advise him on short-cuts and pitfalls. In New York, London and Los Angeles, he can meet plenty of people with money to invest in good ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Bernstam, investors from the US are scared to invest in Germany because they don&rsquo;t understand the way companies are structured and corporate regulations. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t understand what could happen if future investors tried to dilute us,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;and investors from the States don&rsquo;t understand yet how that works abroad &ndash; and are frankly scared of investing abroad.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All of which created a chicken-and-egg problem in Berlin. Some entrepreneurs have stayed away because of the lack of investors, and those with the money have preferred to go elsewhere because of the greater number of companies to invest in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this resistance is beginning breaking down, on both sides, according to David Knight, editor-in-chief of Berlin tech scene blog &lt;a href=\"http://siliconallee.com/\"&gt;Silicon Allee&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s been this streak of creativity and once you got a core of people, which started round 2009, you started to get a lot of investors from the US and Europe looking to Berlin. It&rsquo;s still at a very early stage, but there&rsquo;s a lot more money now than there was two years ago.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Districts such as Kreuzberg, Neukoelln and Prenzlauer Berg, where would-be entrepreneurs congregate in the bars and cafes offering wi-fi, are now seeing the effect of this. In Prenzlauer Berg, &lt;a href=\"http://www.factoryberlin.com/\"&gt;Factory&lt;/a&gt;, which received &euro;1 million in funding from Google, has rented space to over 15 companies, including SoundCloud. The city also now has a lot more of what might be called serious business people. Florian Lanzer is trying to build a website selling green products to people who are not necessarily committed environmentalists. He said the project has attracted a lot of interest, including the involvement of a former CEO of Sony Germany.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;What Berlin gets right is that it attracts the right kind of people,&rdquo; says Lanzer. &ldquo;Young people with talent and people with long track records and massive experience are coming together.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around a decade ago, Berlin was famously described as being &ldquo;poor, but sexy&rdquo; by its mayor, Klaus Wowereit. The hope is that creativity and commerce is now beginning to combine in tech in such a way that it might finally shed its poor tag.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Berlin start-ups: Cool and commerce coming together?","HeadlineShort":"Berlin: Can cool and capital mix?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In a new series Innovation Cultures takes an on-the-ground look at tech scenes around the world that hope in some way to emulate the successes of Silicon Valley. First up is Berlin, where Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"slideshow","SummaryLong":"In the first of a new series looking at global tech scenes, Stephen Evans asks whether the cutting-edge, low-rent city is finally attracting serious investment.","SummaryShort":"Hip start-up city may be coming of age","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"4b0f1c8f-2f5c-472d-8d83-5265b87745f1","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130814-berlin-can-cool-and-capital-mix","_id":"559a5e0c157e584f77e2061d"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"ib2video","AssetVideoIb2":[],"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":null,"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Every government fears the hostile takeover of key infrastructure like transport systems or power stations. But governments themselves are believed to target other nations to search for intelligence, information and data that might be useful.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With experts now saying that security breaches are inevitable, the question is how to minimise the danger and the damage. BBC Click&rsquo;s Dan Simmons visits a major cyber-security company to look behind the scenes at how state-sponsored hacking can be prevented.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;See also: &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20130820-cyber-pearl-harbor-a-real-fear\"&gt;Cyber Pearl Harbor: Why hasn&rsquo;t a mega attack happened?&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;You can also find the Click team on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCClick\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or at &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBCCLICK\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;@BBCClick&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-20T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"How big a threat is state sponsored computer hacking?","HeadlineShort":"Cyber enemy of the state","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Dan Simmons visits a major cyber-security company to look behind the scenes at how, or if, state-sponsored hacking can be prevented.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"Dan Simmons visits a major cyber-security company to look behind the scenes at how, or if, state-sponsored hacking can be prevented.","SummaryShort":"Can state-sponsored hacking be prevented?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"3a44884a-261b-41c0-b53e-c64d635271e3","Id":"wwfuture/story/20130820-cyber-enemy-of-the-state","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20130820-cyber-enemy-of-the-state"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20130820-cyber-enemy-of-the-state","_id":"559a5e0d157e584f77e20637"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Along Lake Eyasi in northern Tanzania I come across the Hadza community sitting in male- and female-only groups, with the men playing small lute-like stringed instruments and applying a pre-hunt poisonous tree resin to their metal arrowheads. They light a small fire by rapidly twisting a hardwood twig into a softwood stick from the local Commiphora tree. It soon smoulders and, intrigued, I have a go too. It's surprisingly difficult, but with their help, I get it to smoke eventually.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;I've travelled four hours west from the city of Arusha to meet this ancient tribe of hunter-gatherers, and join them in a bow-and-arrow hunt for prey among the thorn bushes. The Hadza people may have nothing &ndash; no animals, land or possessions aside from the clothes on their backs &ndash; but they are rich in the skills and resourcefulness they need to produce everything from their environment.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That&rsquo;s not all that sets them apart from most societies. The Hadza are thought to be the most ancient modern humans, the first surviving peoples to have split off from our ancestral family tree, and are not closely related genetically to any other peoples. Their language &ndash; a clicking tongue, also called Hadza &ndash; is unique and unrelated even to other clicking languages. Some linguists believe Hadza may be close to humankind's first ancestral language.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Dying out&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However the language may not be around for long. The Hadza bushmen, who live in groups of around 15 people, are believed to have been living in this remote area for at least 10,000 years, but there are now less than 1,000 Hadza left. Fewer than 400 of them continue to live a stone-age lifestyle &ndash; they are among the last hunter-gatherers in a continent of farmers and pastoralists. The numbers will continue to drop, as their land is swallowed up by farmers, government-designated conservation areas and private game reserves. And their sing-song tongue, punctuated with clicks and glottal stops, and which has no words for numbers past four, is no longer being learned by all Hadza children. As the modern world encroaches, the language is in danger of being lost as Hadza make greater use of the widely spoken Swahili tongue.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What is at risk is not simply the vocabulary and grammar of this unique language, but the Hadza's &ndash; and by extension, part of humanity's &ndash; cultural heritage and expression.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Hadza are not alone in facing the loss of their native tongue. Every 14 days &lt;a href=\"http://www.swarthmore.edu/SocSci/langhotspots/intro.html\"&gt;a language dies&lt;/a&gt;. Over half of the approximately 7,000 languages spoken on the planet may &lt;a href=\"http://travel.nationalgeographic.com/travel/enduring-voices/\"&gt;disappear by the end of the century&lt;/a&gt;. In the age of the &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120209-welcome-to-the-age-of-modern-man\"&gt;Anthropocene&lt;/a&gt;, language extinction is happening faster than species extinction. Eighty percent of the endangered languages are African, including Hadza, and the majority have no written form. Once the last speaker dies, so does the language. And for an oral culture, preserving language becomes even more important for maintaining the identity and heritage of a community.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is said that with every language you speak you gain a new soul. But 80% of the world's population now speak &lt;a href=\"http://www.swarthmore.edu/SocSci/langhotspots/globaltrends.html\"&gt;just 1.1% of its languages&lt;/a&gt;, and universal languages, like English, dominate the internet, signage and the majority of published texts. So, is the Anthropocene becoming a duller place, in which the rich diversity of humanity's full and wonderful language abilities is reduced to a handful of tongues: English, Mandarin, and a few others? Are we in danger of losing our soul?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Not if some people have their way. They are fighting back to preserve rarer tongues - in some cases, with great success.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Unlikely saviour&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps the most successful example is Hebrew, which was dead two centuries ago, before being revived and brought into the 20th century as a living language and the mother tongue of an entire generation of Israelis. Other languages have also been brought back from the brink of extinction through the sheer will and determination of their communities, including Welsh, Cornish, Gaelic and New Zealand Maori.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Language preservation works best when the language, culture and identity of minority-speaker communities are respected by national governments &ndash; rather than being banned, children are either fully taught in their mother tongue or given dedicated classes in it, in addition to learning the national or regional language. Evidence is mounting for the benefits of &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2012/03/18/opinion/sunday/the-benefits-of-bilingualism.html\"&gt;bi- or multi-lingualism&lt;/a&gt;, and for the social and psychological importance of mother-tongue conservation, which allows conversation across multiple generations and strengthens cultural continuity and community identity.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In some places, technology is proving an unlikely saviour. K. David Harrison, a linguist at Swarthmore College in Pennsylvania is creating &lt;a href=\"http://www.swarthmore.edu/watch-linguist-k-david-harrison-describes-efforts-to-help-endangered-languages-survive.xml\"&gt;'talking dictionaries&lt;/a&gt;' of endangered languages that help users to preserve the tongues digitally though audio recordings of native speakers and pictures, and has a &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/user/EnduringVoices\"&gt;YouTube channel&lt;/a&gt; for endangered languages. The languages can then be used more easily for text messages over mobile phones, for example. \"In some cases, people have created an orthography [script] for languages that have never been written down, so they can send SMS messages to each other,\" he says. So far, Harrison has created talking dictionaries in eight languages that have never previously been recorded including ones in Papua New Guinea, Siberia and Paraguay, and has six more dictionaries on the way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of the benefits of the globalised internet, Harrison says, is that remote villagers with endangered languages can reach a global audience. For example, a hiphop duo from a village in Arunachal Pradesh in northeast India, has garnered more than 9,000 global viewers for their &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7epBWBzjjdY&amp;list=FLmbtQpU9Hd-RtiDmdRbHoUw&amp;index=9&amp;feature=plpp_video\"&gt;YouTube video in the Aka language&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; that's more than ten times the number of Aka speakers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Hadza have now also had their language preserved in a dictionary by Kirk Miller, an American linguist who spent eight months living with them. It might be just in time. When we return from the bush hunt, a couple of the Hadza men try to sell me a bow and arrow, while some of the women string beads to sell to tourists. New cash from these enterprises is funding an unhealthy supply of alcohol that is eroding their culture as surely as the Tanzanian government's previous 'civilising' attempts. As communities cease to function productively, and individuals migrate away, fewer and fewer speakers will bear testimony to a way of life that was once practiced by the ancestors of us all.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt;
