German reunification, artists and small companies have been drawn to east Berlin, where rent is remarkably cheap and theres plenty of space. (Copyright: M.a.r.c./Flickr)","SynopsisMedium":"Since German reunification, artists and small companies have been drawn to east Berlin, where rent is remarkably cheap and theres plenty of space. (Copyright: M.a.r.c./Flickr)","SynopsisShort":"Towering strengths","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1f/2y/p01f2yts.jpg","Title":"berlin_flickr_976.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01f2yts","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01f2yts","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01f2yts","_id":"559a5c39157e584f77e1b714"}],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":null,"Description":"","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Stephen Evans","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"75427624-4cb2-4625-b3a9-401b729949af","Id":"wwfuture/author/stephen-evans","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:07Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"stephen-evans"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/stephen-evans","_id":"559a5a55157e584f77e17178"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When new businesses based on the computer chip began to cluster in Santa Clara Valley, it did not take long for someone to come up with the name Silicon Valley. That set a pattern, and soon there was &lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/business/3249891.stm\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Glen&lt;/a&gt; in Scotland, Kenya&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-africa-21173158\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Savannah&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http://www.silicongulf.net/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Gulf&lt;/a&gt; in the Philippines, &lt;a href=\"http://www.academpark.com/en/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Taiga&lt;/a&gt; in Siberia &ndash; even the somewhat less romantic-sounding &lt;a href=\"http://www.siliconroundabout.org.uk/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Silicon Roundabout&lt;/a&gt; in London.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now there is Silicon Allee in Berlin (Allee, meaning alley or avenue in German, is part of many street names in the city), which to some observers evokes the atmosphere of the early days in Northern California. That was certainly the vibe at &lt;a href=\"http://toaberlin.com/\"&gt;Tech Open Air Berlin&lt;/a&gt;, a gathering held earlier this month in a ramshackle complex of wooden and stone buildings in Kreuzberg, a trendy part of the city. There was soft acoustic music and falafel, and a lot of beautiful people exuding a studied coolness.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You&rsquo;re going to see a lot of amazing stuff coming out of Berlin,&rdquo; says Tikhon Bernstam, who came over from Silicon Valley to attend the event. &ldquo;This sort of culture that you see here, the hippy music, we had exactly the same thing in San Francisco a few years ago. I&rsquo;ve been impressed by the quality of the companies I&rsquo;ve been seeing here.&rdquo; Bernstam&rsquo;s cloud data service he co-founded, Parse, was recently bought by Facebook for $85 million.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Of course, Berlin is many leagues behind Silicon Valley. From Adobe through to Yahoo, via Apple, Intel, Google and Oracle, the Valley, as its occupants call it, has a combined wealth which most countries would envy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exact figures are hard to come by, but Berlin came ninth in &lt;a href=\"http://www.theatlanticcities.com/jobs-and-economy/2013/06/new-global-start-cities/5144/\"&gt;a recent global ranking&lt;/a&gt; of cities with the most tech start-ups founded since 2002 that are global, or received angel funding or institutional venture capital. The top three spots were occupied by San Francisco-Silicon Valley with 201 start-ups, New York (144) and London (90). Berlin had 27, just ahead of Bangalore on 26 and Sao Paulo on 21.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But with its reputation for doing things differently, very cheap rents, and the ambition and optimism on show at events like Tech Open Air Berlin, the hope is that its scene will deliver on the buzz that has surrounded it for the past few years.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The city itself is in many ways a start up. From 1945 it was shattered and split for almost half a century. The eastern, Soviet-controlled zone was hardly a seed-bed for innovation. Industry moved out from the West. Students were exempted from military service, attracting a young, artistic and politically radical alternative scene.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Since the fall of the Wall in 1989, the city has been re-inventing itself. The vibrant alternative culture remains in place, and some see it as providing fertile ground for the creation of disruptive technologies &ndash; though others argue that a counterculture-led antipathy to money-making may hamper innovation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One thing that is clear is that property is cheap &ndash; Berlin still has rents which are so low that they make visitors from London and New York gasp. The average annual cost of a workstation in an office in Berlin, including maintenance costs, taxes and rent, was $8,410 at the end of last year, compared with $14,050 for New York and $14,620 in the City of London, according to &lt;a href=\"http://dtz.b15media.com/downloads/DTZ_Occupier_Perspective_Global_Occupancy_Costs_Offices_2013.pdf\"&gt;a report&lt;/a&gt; published by the global property company DTZ. Renting a 120 square metre apartment in Berlin costs around a third what it does in London and New York.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This combination has attracted many who aspire to be the next big thing in tech. In a few cases they have already succeeded, such as the social game developer &lt;a href=\"http://www.wooga.com/\"&gt;Wooga&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\"http://www.researchgate.net/\"&gt;Research Gate&lt;/a&gt;, a networking website for scientists. The darling of the scene is &lt;a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/\"&gt;SoundCloud&lt;/a&gt;, an audio sharing platform that has 200 million users every month. Alex Ljung, its co-founder and chief executive, moved the company from Stockholm to Berlin because he felt it offered a richer mix of people involved in music, and because of the ample and cheap office space.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, Ljung admits his firm&rsquo;s new hometown doesn&rsquo;t cover all bases. &ldquo;With start-up life, there&rsquo;s never really much calmness. It&rsquo;s all jet and no lag,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;In an average month, I spend about a week-and-a-half in Berlin, about a week in San Francisco and the rest of the time split between Los Angeles, New York and London.&rdquo; What Berlin lacks that San Francisco has is the sheer volume of people who&rsquo;ve grown tech companies, where Ljung says he can bump into others who have trodden the same path, and who can advise him on short-cuts and pitfalls. In New York, London and Los Angeles, he can meet plenty of people with money to invest in good ideas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to Bernstam, investors from the US are scared to invest in Germany because they don&rsquo;t understand the way companies are structured and corporate regulations. &ldquo;We don&rsquo;t understand what could happen if future investors tried to dilute us,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;and investors from the States don&rsquo;t understand yet how that works abroad &ndash; and are frankly scared of investing abroad.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;All of which created a chicken-and-egg problem in Berlin. Some entrepreneurs have stayed away because of the lack of investors, and those with the money have preferred to go elsewhere because of the greater number of companies to invest in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But this resistance is beginning breaking down, on both sides, according to David Knight, editor-in-chief of Berlin tech scene blog &lt;a href=\"http://siliconallee.com/\"&gt;Silicon Allee&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s been this streak of creativity and once you got a core of people, which started round 2009, you started to get a lot of investors from the US and Europe looking to Berlin. It&rsquo;s still at a very early stage, but there&rsquo;s a lot more money now than there was two years ago.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Districts such as Kreuzberg, Neukoelln and Prenzlauer Berg, where would-be entrepreneurs congregate in the bars and cafes offering wi-fi, are now seeing the effect of this. In Prenzlauer Berg, &lt;a href=\"http://www.factoryberlin.com/\"&gt;Factory&lt;/a&gt;, which received &euro;1 million in funding from Google, has rented space to over 15 companies, including SoundCloud. The city also now has a lot more of what might be called serious business people. Florian Lanzer is trying to build a website selling green products to people who are not necessarily committed environmentalists. He said the project has attracted a lot of interest, including the involvement of a former CEO of Sony Germany.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;What Berlin gets right is that it attracts the right kind of people,&rdquo; says Lanzer. &ldquo;Young people with talent and people with long track records and massive experience are coming together.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Around a decade ago, Berlin was famously described as being &ldquo;poor, but sexy&rdquo; by its mayor, Klaus Wowereit. The hope is that creativity and commerce is now beginning to combine in tech in such a way that it might finally shed its poor tag.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Facebook&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;span&gt;Twitter&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a5a69157e584f77e17491"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-14T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Berlin start-ups: Cool and commerce coming together?","HeadlineShort":"Berlin: Can cool and capital mix?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"In a new series Innovation Cultures takes an on-the-ground look at tech scenes around the world that hope in some way to emulate the successes of Silicon Valley. In most cities, these spaces offer students, programmers, developers, entrepreneurs, creative, investors and techies a place to work, network and create for the first time.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As these spaces become the nerve centres for the tech community, they also become critical international touch-points for those seeking to engage in technology and business in Africa. By implementing ideas based on this information, the company hopes to keep staff happy, engaged and productive.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Click's Sumi Das reports on whether it is possible for Google to perfect the science that links employee satisfaction to workplace efficiency.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; Like many Greeks these days, he calls the economic crisis his country is going through \"an obsession,\" particularly for people in their mid-30s.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"We have to fight for three generations,\" Stampoulidis tells me. \"We fight for ourselves, and for our kids. We don't want to hand over a country that's collapsing to them. And we also have to fight for our parents, because people on pensions were the first to take the heat when the crisis came.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Such stories are part of the fabric of life in Greece these days. Jobs have been axed, wages and pensions slashed, and social safety nets have almost completely broken down. Homelessness and suicides are both on the increase. Young people, particularly those who are educated and talented, seem to be crowding the exits, looking for the next flight out. And despite &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-17390450\"&gt;the approval of the latest bailout&lt;/a&gt;, many Greeks feel that the worst is yet to come.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But Stampoulidis and his business partner, Efstratios Kehayas, are bullish on Greece, especially when it comes to the role that technology might play in helping in the country&rsquo;s recovery. The two entrepreneurs, who met while completing their PhDs at Greece's National Technical University in Athens, founded a company called &lt;a href=\"http://www.constelex.eu/\"&gt;Constelex&lt;/a&gt; a few years ago.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Constelex makes light amplifiers for fiber-optic networks. Giving the light a boost and extending the range of the network, the co-founders tell me, has applications in telecommunications, remote sensing, aerospace and medicine.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Both Stampoulidis and Kehayas could have taken their knowledge and skills and gone elsewhere to look for work, or start a company. Like many of their friends, they could have left Greece's economic woes, its stifling bureaucracy and corruption, far behind. But they did not.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"For us, it was a no-brainer,\" Kehayas says. \"We wanted to build something in our country, and then offer something back in the form of new jobs, and new products that can be exported.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Cluster feed&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That is music to the ears of Vassilios Makios, Professor Emeritus at the University of Patras, and General Director of &lt;a href=\"http://www.corallia.org/el.html\"&gt;Corallia&lt;/a&gt;, the Hellenic Technology Clusters Initiative. Makios himself left Greece in the 1960s to study electrical engineering in Germany. For years after, he taught at Carleton University in Ottawa before returning to Greece in 1978.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;His time in North America convinced him that he needed to kick-start technology entrepreneurship in Greece. In the ensuing years, he sent more than 600 Greek students abroad in the hope that they would return to Greece with new ideas about technology and how to do business.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He watched new Greek tech companies spring up, only to fail because of funding problems, bad business plans or because they failed to patent their ideas. What these start-ups needed, Makios thought, was a little bit of a push in the right direction, and a whole lot more cooperation.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And so, in 2006, he joined forces with two other Greeks who had worked at the famous &lt;a href=\"http://www.alcatel-lucent.com/wps/portal/belllabs\"&gt;Bell Labs&lt;/a&gt;, and asked for some seed money from the Greek government and the European Union to start Corallia. He is now at the forefront of a push to get the Greek government, and budding Greek engineers and technologists, to see the value in clustering, an idea popularized by &lt;a href=\"http://drfd.hbs.edu/fit/public/facultyInfo.do?facInfo=bio&amp;facEmId=mporter\"&gt;Michael Porter at Harvard Business School&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The idea is pretty straightforward: you put groups of complementary start-ups together in the same building, and they all start feeding off one another to develop new ideas and new products. In 2006, Corallia, with that seed money from the Greek government and the European Union, tried this clustering idea with 13 micro-electronics companies in Greece.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are now more than 60 companies and 30 universities involved in the micro-electronics cluster. Together, they are responsible for more than $260m in exports, according to Makios. The products range from sensor networks and mobile technology to solar energy equipment and micro-chips for studying DNA.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Corallia itself is a non-profit. It leases the building, and rents out spaces to start-ups. It provides these small companies with internet connections, teleconferencing facilities and meeting rooms. There are in-house experts who can give companies advice about filing patents, making business plans, and pitching venture capitalists.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"I call it a facilitator for the high-tech sector,\" says Costas Meimitis, CEO of a small start-up called &lt;a href=\"http://www.antcor.com/\"&gt;Antcor&lt;/a&gt;, which licences wi-fi software for the chip industry. Antcor only has 12 employees, and Meimitis says that if they had to deal with IP attorneys, accountants and auditors they wouldn't have to time to work on their technology.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"Corallia's help puts me in the right mind-set,\" he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Death warrant&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It is people like Meimitis &ndash; along with help from universities and Corallia -&nbsp; that Makios believes can &ldquo;change Greece&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&nbsp;\"Yes, Greece is in a difficult situation right now, a real mess, but I'm confident that we can come out of this mess because of these technology prospects,\" he says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"This cluster,\" Makios emphasizes, \"will create a small, but positive, Silicon Valley in Greece.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, the promise of a \"Silicon Valley for Greece\" might be stretching it a bit. Economist and venture capitalist Aristos Doxiadis does, on balance, think that tech can be a viable sector in the new Greek economy, but he says that he doesn't \"see a huge number of jobs being created,\" at least in the short term.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"The fundamentals, and by that I mean the people, are here,\" Doxiadis says. \"But the bureaucratic obstacles are still immense.\" Add to that, Doxiadis tells me, that a whole generation of Greeks is still coming to terms with the fact the decades-old system of steady, government jobs with inflated wages and generous pensions is crumbling.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"There is no free lunch anymore,\" Doxiadis says. \"We have to figure out how to be productive.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And that, says Vassilios Makios, is exactly the message he's giving to Greek students coming out of university these days.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"I tell them this:&nbsp; 'If at the age of 25, you want to become a civil servant, they already have a coffin ready for you. You're dead.'\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was a message that resonated strongly with Constalex's Efstratios Kehayas. \"Personally, I decided that I wanted to build things, that I wanted to mingle with top researchers and entrepreneurs. I wanted the rush. If you put me in an office, and made me do the same thing over and over for 25 years, that would kill me.\"&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Constelex got its first sales, and its first patent, last year and the company is now working on a way to modify its technology to better treat cancer.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As for Corallia, it has started two new clusters, one for start-ups working on technologies related to space, and the other in gaming. There is no reason, Vassilios Makios says, that the same idea can't apply to other industries such as bio-engineering, or even to Greek wine-makers.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;\"We can brand it all 'made in Greece,'\" he says, beaming. \"And make it a standard around the world.\"&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","HeadlineLong":"Greek target=\"_self\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;@BBCClick&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","HeadlineLong":"Turning on to technology","HeadlineShort":"Turning on to technology","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The French city of Bordeaux has been trying to turn on those who are normally turned cold by technology. \n","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":[],"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"BBC Click visits a French city using technology to help everyone from skaters to start-ups.","SummaryShort":"The French city using technology to help everyone from skaters to start-ups","Tag":null},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"2b8db957-62f3-4c72-98e1-daebcb74a9b0","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120405-turning-on-to-technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120405-turning-on-to-technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120405-turning-on-to-technology","_id":"559a5ddf157e584f77e2005b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"&lt;iframe width=\"624\" height=\"351\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/9q37tXUAjDw?rel=0\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"customEmbed","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;One person&rsquo;s insult can become another&rsquo;s badge of pride. In religion, words like Methodist, Mormon and Quaker first appeared as disparaging terms, and were then taken up in defiance by believers. In the politics of gender and sexuality, words like &ldquo;suffragette&rdquo;, &ldquo;queer&rdquo; and &ldquo;dyke&rdquo; have been re-appropriated by those they were coined to abuse.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The word &ldquo;geek&rdquo; is less ideologically loaded, but it too came from the lowest levels of society. Arriving in English from low German, a &lt;em&gt;geck &lt;/em&gt;originally described a fool or crazy person. The term probably migrated to America via European travelling circuses, where it was used first to refer to gullible punters. By the early 20th Century, the phrase referred to the most outlandish performers and exhibits in carnivals &ndash; and to an outsider even among these outsiders, a \"wild man\" chasing and biting the heads off live chickens.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Exactly how this insult escaped carnival culture and became generic is mysterious (although William Gresham&rsquo;s carnival-set 1946 novel &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nightmare_Alley\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Nightmare Alley&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, &lt;/em&gt;and subsequent film, had a lot to do with it). But, by the 1980s, the arrival of geeks as we know them had been cemented in iconic style by the coming-of-age film &lt;em&gt;Sixteen Candles&lt;/em&gt; (1984), courtesy of a character known as &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://www.denofgeek.com/movies/14472/celebrating-john-hughes-sixteen-candles\"&gt;The Geek&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; whose brainy awkwardness set the mould for an era.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Geeks &ndash; like their cousins in etymological oddity, &lt;a href=\"http://etymonline.com/?term=nerd\"&gt;nerds&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; were the pitiable extremes of an era in which only terminally under-confident males were obsessed with computers. Yet, as the realm of computing bred its first generation of millionaires and then billionaires, the unthinkable has happened: geeks have become not only acceptable, but even &lt;a href=\"http://sogeekchic.com/\"&gt;chic&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; to the extent that YouTube is hosting its first &lt;a href=\"http://www.youtube.com/user/YouTube\"&gt;Geek Week&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Not everyone will admit to geekish tendencies, but the screens on every desk and in every pocket say differently: love or loathe the word, almost all of us have some element of geekery in our lives. Indeed, given that almost every conceivable obsession now has a presence online, &ldquo;geek&rdquo; is as much a label of enthusiasm as of expertise. You might be a &lt;a href=\"http://coffeegeek.com/\"&gt;coffee geek&lt;/a&gt;, a &lt;a href=\"http://boardgamegeek.com/\"&gt;board games geek&lt;/a&gt;, or a &lt;a href=\"http://www.redbubble.com/shop/geek+retro+t-shirts\"&gt;retro-geek fashionista&lt;/a&gt;. Or you might get your geek on for &lt;a href=\"http://www.denofgeek.com/tags/doctor-who\"&gt;Doctor Who&lt;/a&gt;, following liveblogs and hashtags covering the announcement of Peter Capaldi as being the 12th doctor, or creating a whole new genre of music, &lt;a href=\"http://www.urbandictionary.com/define.php?term=Trock\"&gt;Trock&lt;/a&gt;, dedicated to the Gallifreyan time lord &ndash; and in the process earning its originator Alex Day the opportunity to visit the real Tardis (video above).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No matter what you&rsquo;re into, it seems that courtesy of modern technology you&rsquo;ll never be alone for long. Like-minded others are only a click or a search-term away. And so too are whole realms of sharing, comparison, collaboration, one-upmanship, status anxiety, and the sheer timewasting gloriousness of typing &ldquo;check this out!&rdquo; next to a YouTube link.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lsquo;Geekocratic moment&rsquo;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For many people, such enthusiasm &ndash; even over seemingly trivial issues &ndash; is a road towards cultural participation and creation, and perhaps other forms of engagement as well. The screen is a lot more forgiving than face-to-face first encounters, and shared enthusiasm can be a powerful binding force.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet, at the same time there are good reasons to be &ldquo;lovingly suspicious of geek culture&rdquo;, as the author China Mieville put it when I &lt;a href=\"http://boingboing.net/2012/05/31/an-interview-with-china-mievil.html\"&gt;interviewed him last year&lt;/a&gt; about the business of writing cult fiction in the 21st Century.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On Wikipedia, articles about &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_wars\"&gt;Star Wars&lt;/a&gt; or &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_trek\"&gt;Star Trek&lt;/a&gt; are often better referenced, longer and more passionately debated than those that are more important by almost any sense of worldly value, such as events during the Second World War, the lives of major historical figures, or even religions (and that&rsquo;s before you start &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_YouTube_celebrities\"&gt;listing YouTube celebrities&lt;/a&gt;).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ours is, as Mieville notes, a &ldquo;geekocratic moment&rdquo;. Shared passions and ideas have a curious force of their own online, and even those who adore this will admit that this can lead to warped priorities &ndash; from staggeringly brutal &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flaming_(Internet)\"&gt;flame wars&lt;/a&gt; to trolling and &lt;a href=\"http://www.thisview.org/?p=127\"&gt;deeply engrained sexism&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then there&rsquo;s the triumph of the geeks themselves: not only the new hordes of enthusiasts, but the &lt;a href=\"http://www.martinfowler.com/bliki/AlphaGeek.html\"&gt;alpha geeks&lt;/a&gt; whose creations drive much of our online world. From Facebook&rsquo;s Mark Zuckerberg to Google&rsquo;s Larry Page and Sergey Brin (not to mention the late Steve Jobs, perhaps the closest thing to a &lt;a href=\"http://www.saintstevejobs.com/\"&gt;patron saint&lt;/a&gt; the cult of geek has so far enshrined), technocrats are shaping the texture of our lives as never before. And this brings its own species of hazards.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ours is an age of big data, and even bigger surveillance &ndash; of unprecedented information freedom matched to the unprecedented dominance of a few global players who own the &ldquo;ecosystems&rdquo; through which this information flows. Each of us is free to indulge our passions and predilections as never before &ndash; yet the channels through which we do this can be perilously narrow, and our perspective on the world correspondingly warped.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At least, this is what can happen &ndash; but it need not be an inexorable fate. Online, you&rsquo;re never alone, and perhaps the greatest gift of this fact is that you&rsquo;re never more than a few screens away from someone who has been there before, answered the question, questioned the assumption, solved the problem &ndash; or edited the &lt;a href=\"http://starwars.wikia.com/wiki/Main_Page\"&gt;Wookieepedia&lt;/a&gt;. Your passions and distractions are yours to negotiate, in tandem with whomsoever you can enlist along the way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Once upon a time, geeks were freaks in the most literal sense: humans rendered sub-human, performing in front of a baying crowd. Outposts of the internet can still feel like that today. Yet, in our geekocratic moment, something fundamental has shifted. Nobody is outside the crowd, and nothing human is entirely strange. This is far from an unqualified cause for celebration &ndash; but it&rsquo;s much too late to turn back the clock.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook page&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2013-08-05T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Social media, Doctor Who and the rise of the geeks","HeadlineShort":"The unstoppable rise of the geeks","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Thanks to the fact that like-minded souls are only a click or a search-term away, the unthinkable has happened  geeks have become not only acceptable, but even chic.              <div class="bottom-unit">
              <div class="body-content">
               <p>
                Rothschild Boulevard has always been one of the grandest streets in Tel Aviv, with its broad tree-lined central promenade and elegant Bauhaus architecture.
               </p>
               <p>
                The cafes, kiosks and benches teem with young entrepreneurs who seem to survive on a diet of caffeine and wi-fi. Of Israels 5,000 tech companies, city planners have counted 600 start-ups in the single square mile around Rothschild. Israel has more companies on Nasdaq than any country beside the US and China, and it attracts more venture funding per capita than anywhere in the world. Its tech sector employs 230,000 people in a population of eight million, earning more than a quarter of the countrys total exports.
               </p>
               <p>
                Just off the southern end of the boulevard is the Shalom Meir Tower  once the tallest building in the Middle East. A lift door in the corner of the mezzanine leads to a municipal library on the seventh floor that had been forgotten by all but its ageing clientele. In 2011, it was refurbished as a shared workspace for start-ups. The fourth group of 13 companies has just finished their four-month session, which includes meetings with potential partners, lectures by business experts, networking  not to mention a great view over the city's beach.
               </p>
               <p>
                The pattern is repeated all the way down Rothschild, where incubators and start-ups compete for space among the coffee shops and restaurants. There are similar tech hubs in many cities throughout the world, but Avner Warner, Director of International Economic Development at the
                <a href="http://www.tel-aviv.gov.il/eng/GlobalCity/Pages/GlobalCityLoby.aspx?tm=24">
                 Tel Aviv Global City Administration
                </a>
                , says Tel Aviv stands apart because of a single overriding influence on early-stage Israeli companies: the Israeli armed forces.
               </p>
               <p>
                The military gives them their technological experience, managerial experience and networks, says Warner. For the average person, the greatest barrier to being an entrepreneur is 'Where the hell do I start? What do I do first?' Most people just get confused at this point and go get a normal job. Thats the experience the army gives them. Its understanding how you actually build a product, which is the same as managing a project in the army.
               </p>
               <p>
                <strong>
                 Big data pioneers
                </strong>
               </p>
               <p>
                Inside the HQ of the Mamram, the Israel Defence Forces (IDF) technical support unit in nearby Ramat Gan, computer training course commander Capt. H (her full name is classified) says new recruits on a six-month intensive programming course study from dawn till night and are taught programming skills, teamwork, project management and  most importantly how to be creative. Its like a school for start-ups.
               </p>
               <p>
                When you do a degree in computer science you study the technical things," she says. "You study how to write a code, mathematics. We dont focus on that. We focus on how to work in a team. How to understand what your client needs and make software that fits his demands. How to write good code that you will be able to de-bug and maintain."
               </p>
               <p>
                Tal Marian, founder of the
                <a href="http://www.techloft.co.il/">
                 TechLoft
                </a>
                , a commercial shared workspace just off Rothschild, says the results of the military training are obvious. Some of the military units work like a civilian organisation," says Marian. "They encourage entrepreneurship, the feeling that if you come up with a good idea that answers a real need of that units mission, you will get the funding and manpower and the time you need."
               </p>
               <p>
                After years of helping to solve the nations major security threats, the challenges of gaming and mobile apps pale by comparison, he adds.
               </p>
               <p>
                Sometimes, the military technology connection is obvious. The miniaturised camera and power pack in the
                <a href="http://www.givenimaging.com/en-us/Pages/default.aspx">
                 Given Imaging camera pill
                </a>
                is based on equipment in the nose of a military drone, for example. Israel raced a decade ahead in the management and use of large data sets  or big data  that drive many consumer apps because of expertisedeveloped by
                <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_8200">
                 Unit 8200
                </a>
                , the intelligence-gathering arm that is now the largest unit in the IDF.
               </p>
               <p>
                IBM has spent nearly $1bn (640m) on four Israeli companies that all developed big data storage solutions: Storwize, XIV, Diligent and FileX. Earlier this year, Google acquired the mapping app
                <a href="http://www.waze.com/">
                 Waze
                </a>
                for more than $1 bn.
               </p>
               <p>
                The founders of Waze received their technical training in Israeli military intelligence. The giant Israeli tech firms
                <a href="http://www.nice.com/">
                 Nice
                </a>
                ,
                <a href="http://www.comverse.com/index.asp">
                 Comverse
                </a>
                and
                <a href="http://www.checkpoint.com/">
                 Check Point
                </a>
                were all created by Unit 8200 alumni or based on technology originally developed by the unit.
               </p>
               <p>
                <strong>
                 Youthful exuberance
                </strong>
               </p>
               <p>
                Across the boulevard, Yaron Tal is Chief Technical Officer of 6Scan, a website security start-up hefounded with ex-army buddies. A white-hat hacker (one that works for non-malicious motives), since the age of 12, he was running his own web security consultancy by the age of 17, before being head-hunted for Matzov, thearmys cyber-security unit.
               </p>
               <p>
                Entrepreneurs in Israel are unique, he says. Their approach to problems is different to others because the army is a huge incubator for innovation and entrepreneurship. The army gave us a few million dollars at the age of 18 and asked us to build technology and systems that address problems that only people 10 or 20 years older are dealing with in other parts of the world. That kind of pressure and challenge really brings a lot of things out of you.
               </p>
               <p>
                Tal skipped university to work at a start-up before launching his own, but another important driver of the tech scene is the fact that Israeli university students pay only about $3,100 (2,000) a year in tuition fees. They emerge from military service and three years of studying with zero debt, eager to take a year off to pursue their dreams.
               </p>
               <p>
                That youthful exuberance, combined with the rigorous military training in technology and project management, has found a natural home among cafes running down the centre of Rothschild.
               </p>
               <p>
                Recruiting to 6Scan became easy after they opened an office on the boulevard, according to Tal. The street is full of start-ups. We talk together, help each other. There are no big companies here, which is great. We live very near the office. We love the vibe, the place, the other start-ups, the restaurants. Its easier to bring developers to the company when you say its in Rothschild even though we dont have much money to offer. We offer them equity and a good place to spend each day.
               </p>
               <p>
                <em>
                 If you would like to comment on this article or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our
                </em>
                <a href="https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture">

                </a>
                <em>

                </em>
                <a href="https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future">

                 </em>
                </a>
                <em>
                 .
                </em>
               </p>
               <p>
               </p>

  <script type="text/javascript">
   document.write('&lt;' + 'script id="orb-js-script" data-assetpath="http://static.bbci.co.uk/frameworks/barlesque/2.83.10/orb/4/" src="http://static.bbci.co.uk/frameworks/barlesque/2.83.10/orb/4/script' + (( document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_debugorbjs') &gt; -1 )? '-debug' : '') + '/orb.js"&gt;&lt;' + '/script&gt;'); &lt;/script&gt;  &lt;script type="text/javascript"&gt; (function() {
    'use strict';

    var promoManager = {
        url: '',
        segments: ['a', 'b'],
        promoLoaded: false,
                makeUrl: function (variant, theme, win) {
            var loc = win? win.location : window.location,
                proto = loc.protocol,
                host = loc.host,
                url = proto + '//' + ((proto.match(/s:/i) &amp;&amp; !host.match(/^www\.(int|test)\./i))? 'ssl.' : 'www.'),
                themes = ['light', 'dark'];

            if ( host.match(/^(?:www|ssl)\.(int|test|stage|live)\.bbc\./i) ) {
                url += RegExp.$1 + '.';
            }
            else if ( host.match(/^pal\.sandbox\./i) ) {
                url += 'test.';
            }
 
                        theme = themes[ +(theme === themes[0]) ];
           
           return url + 'bbc.co.uk/navpromo/' + variant + '/' + theme;
        },
                validSegment: function (segment) {
            var validSegments = this.segments;
            
            for (var i = 0, len = validSegments.length; i &lt; len; i++) {
                if (validSegments[i] === segment) {
                    return segment;
                }
            }
            
            return validSegments[0];
        },
                init: function(node) {
            var disabledByCookie = (document.cookie.indexOf('ckns_orb_nopromo=1') &gt; -1),
                orbFullWidth     = (document.getElementById('orb-aside').offsetWidth &gt;= 1008),
                that = this;
            
            if (window.promomanagerOverride) {
                for (var p in promomanagerOverride) {
                    that[p] = promomanagerOverride[p];
                }
            }
                
            if ( window.orb.fig('uk') &amp;&amp; orbFullWidth &amp;&amp; !disabledByCookie ) {
                require(['orb/async/_footerpromo', 'istats-1'], function(promo, istats) {
                                        var mandolinEndDate = new Date().getTime() + (7 * 60 * 60 * 24) * 1000,
                        mandolin = new bbc.Mandolin('footer-promo', that.segments, {rate: 0.2, end: mandolinEndDate}),
                        segmentToRequest = that.validSegment(mandolin.getSegment());

                    that.url = (window.promomanagerOverride || that).makeUrl(segmentToRequest, 'light');

                    if (that.url) { 
                        promo.load(that.url, node, {
                                                          onSuccess: function(e) {
                                istats.addLabels({ 'campaignID': e.campaignID });
                                if (segmentToRequest === mandolin.getSegment()) {
                                    istats.addLabels({ 'promo_id_segment': e.campaignID + ':' + mandolin.getSegment() });
                                }
                                istats.track('internal', {region: node, linkLocation : 'orb-footer-promo'});

                                istats.log('display', 'orb-footer-promo-displayed', {campaignID : e.campaignID, testVariant: segmentToRequest});
                                node.className = node.className + ' orb-footer-promo-loaded';
                                promoManager.promoLoaded = true;
                                promoManager.event('promo-loaded').fire(e);
                             },
                             onError: function() {
                                istats.log('error', 'orb-footer-promo-failed');
                                document.cookie = 'ckns_orb_nopromo=1; expires=' + new Date(new Date().getTime() + 1000 * 60 * 10).toGMTString() + ';';
                             }
                        });   
                    }
                });
            }
        }
    };
    
        
    define('orb/promomanager', ['orb/lib/_event'], function (event) {
        event.mixin(promoManager);
        return promoManager;
    });
    
    require(['orb/promomanager'], function (promoManager) {
        promoManager.init(document.getElementById('orb-footer-promo'));
    })
})();
  </script>
